            Once a client has an active pointer grab, pointer events are directed according to the grab rules rather than normal window picking. Typical uses are
            \begin{itemize}
                \item menus
                \item drag-and-drop
                \item resizing a window
                \item moving a window
                \item modal interactions
                \item screen lockers
            \end{itemize}
            For example, while dragging a scrollbar, you do not want pointer events to suddenly go to another window just because the pointer moved outside the scrollbar. A pointer grab lets the scrollbar continue receiving motion/release events until the drag finishes.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            You release grabs with:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            XUngrabPointer(display, time)
            XUngrabKeyboard(display, time)
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            A passive grab does not take effect immediately. Instead, it activates when some input pattern occurs.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            For keyboard shortcuts:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                int XGrabKey(
                    Display* display,
                    int keycode,
                    unsigned int modifiers,
                    Window grab_window,
                    Bool owner_events,
                    int pointer_mode,
                    int keyboard_mode
                );
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item display	Specifies the connection to the X server.
                \item keycode	Specifies the KeyCode or \textbf{AnyKey.}
                \item modifiers	Specifies the set of keymasks or \textbf{AnyModifier.} The mask is the bitwise inclusive OR of the valid keymask bits.
                \item grab\_window	Specifies the grab window.
                \item owner\_events	Specifies a Boolean value that indicates whether the keyboard events are to be reported as usual.
                \item pointer\_mode	Specifies further processing of pointer events. You can pass \textbf{GrabModeSync} or \textbf{GrabModeAsync.}
                \item keyboard\_mode	Specifies further processing of keyboard events. You can pass \textbf{GrabModeSync} or \textbf{GrabModeAsync}
            \end{itemize}
            The \textbf{XGrabKey()} function establishes a passive grab on the keyboard. \textbf{XGrabKey()} can generate \textbf{BadAccess} , \textbf{BadValue} , and \textbf{BadWindow} errors.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            For mouse buttons:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                int XGrabButton(
                    Display* display,
                    unsigned int button,
                    unsigned int modifiers,
                    Window grab_window,
                    Bool owner_events,
                    unsigned int event_mask,
                    int pointer_mode,
                    int keyboard_mode,
                    Window confine_to,
                    Cursor cursor
                );
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item display	Specifies the connection to the X server.
                \item button	Specifies the pointer button that is to be grabbed or \textbf{AnyButton.}
                \item modifiers	Specifies the set of keymasks or \textbf{AnyModifier.} The mask is the bitwise inclusive OR of the valid keymask bits.
                \item grab\_window	Specifies the grab window.
                \item owner\_events	Specifies a Boolean value that indicates whether the pointer events are to be reported as usual or reported with respect to the grab window if selected by the event mask.
                \item event\_mask	Specifies which pointer events are reported to the client. The mask is the bitwise inclusive OR of the valid pointer event mask bits.
                \item pointer\_mode	Specifies further processing of pointer events. You can pass \textbf{GrabModeSync} or \textbf{GrabModeAsync.}
                \item keyboard\_mode	Specifies further processing of keyboard events. You can pass \textbf{GrabModeSync} or \textbf{GrabModeAsync.}
                \item confine\_to	Specifies the window to confine the pointer in or None.
                \item cursor	Specifies the cursor that is to be displayed or None.
            \end{itemize}
            The XGrabButton() function establishes a passive grab. \textbf{XGrabButton()} can generate \textbf{BadCursor,} \textbf{BadValue,} and \textbf{BadWindow} errors.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Xlib defines constants for the standard mouse buttons:
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textbf{Button1}: Typically the left mouse button.
                \item \textbf{Button2}: Typically the middle mouse button (or scroll wheel click).
                \item \textbf{Button3}: Typically the right mouse button.
                \item \textbf{Button4 / Button5}: Often assigned to the scroll wheel up/down actions.
                \item \textbf{AnyButton}: A special constant used in functions like XGrabButton to indicate that the operation should apply to any mouse button
            \end{itemize}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            This is how a window manager can say:
            \begin{center}
                "\textit{When Mod1 + Button1 is pressed over this window, give me control.}"
            \end{center}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The grab becomes active only when the specified key/button/modifier combination occurs.
        \item \textbf{Keymask modifiers}: In Xlib, keymask modifiers are bitwise constants used to describe the state of modifier keys (like Shift, Control, or Alt) during an input event. These masks are commonly found in the state member of event structures such as XKeyEvent or used as arguments in functions like \textbf{XGrabKey.}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textbf{ShiftMask}: The Shift key is pressed.
                \item \textbf{LockMask}: The Caps Lock key is active.
                \item \textbf{ControlMask}: The Control key is pressed.
                \item \textbf{Mod1Mask}: Usually mapped to Alt or Meta.
                \item \textbf{Mod2Mask}: Often mapped to Num Lock.
                \item \textbf{Mod3Mask}: Sometimes mapped to AltGr.
                \item \textbf{Mod4Mask}: Commonly mapped to the Super/Windows key.
                \item \textbf{Mod5Mask}: Often mapped to Scroll Lock or other custom modifiers.
            \end{itemize}
        \item \textbf{Synchronous vs asynchronous grabs}: Grabs can freeze or not freeze event processing. The modes are usually:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            GrabModeAsync
            GrabModeSync
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            With GrabModeAsync, events continue normally. With GrabModeSync, the server may freeze pointer or keyboard event processing until the grabbing client explicitly allows it to continue with:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                int XAllowEvents(
                    Display* display, 
                    int mode, 
                    Time time
                );
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item display	Specifies the connection to the X server.
                \item event\_mode	Specifies the event mode. You can pass \textbf{AsyncPointer,} \textbf{SyncPointer,} \textbf{AsyncKeyboard,} \textbf{SyncKeyboard,} \textbf{ReplayPointer,} \textbf{ReplayKeyboard,} \textbf{AsyncBoth,} or \textbf{SyncBoth.}
                \item time	Specifies the time. You can pass either a timestamp or CurrentTime.
            \end{itemize}
            The XAllowEvents() function releases some queued events if the client has caused a device to freeze. It has no effect if the specified time is earlier than the last-grab time of the most recent active grab for the client or if the specified time is later than the current X server time. Depending on the event\_mode argument, the following occurs:
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textbf{AsyncPointer}:	If the pointer is frozen by the client, pointer event processing continues as usual. If the pointer is frozen twice by the client on behalf of two separate grabs, AsyncPointer thaws for both. AsyncPointer has no effect if the pointer is not frozen by the client, but the pointer need not be grabbed by the client.
                \item \textbf{SyncPointer}:	If the pointer is frozen and actively grabbed by the client, pointer event processing continues as usual until the next ButtonPress or ButtonRelease event is reported to the client. At this time, the pointer again appears to freeze. However, if the reported event causes the pointer grab to be released, the pointer does not freeze. SyncPointer has no effect if the pointer is not frozen by the client or if the pointer is not grabbed by the client.
                \item \textbf{ReplayPointer}:	If the pointer is actively grabbed by the client and is frozen as the result of an event having been sent to the client (either from the activation of a XGrabButton() or from a previous XAllowEvents() with mode SyncPointer but not from a XGrabPointer()), the pointer grab is released and that event is completely reprocessed. This time, however, the function ignores any passive grabs at or above (towards the root of) the grab\_window of the grab just released. The request has no effect if the pointer is not grabbed by the client or if the pointer is not frozen as the result of an event.
                \item \textbf{AsyncKeyboard}:	If the keyboard is frozen by the client, keyboard event processing continues as usual. If the keyboard is frozen twice by the client on behalf of two separate grabs, AsyncKeyboard thaws for both. AsyncKeyboard has no effect if the keyboard is not frozen by the client, but the keyboard need not be grabbed by the client.
                \item \textbf{SyncKeyboard}:	If the keyboard is frozen and actively grabbed by the client, keyboard event processing continues as usual until the next KeyPress or KeyRelease event is reported to the client. At this time, the keyboard again appears to freeze. However, if the reported event causes the keyboard grab to be released, the keyboard does not freeze. SyncKeyboard has no effect if the keyboard is not frozen by the client or if the keyboard is not grabbed by the client.
                \item \textbf{ReplayKeyboard}:	If the keyboard is actively grabbed by the client and is frozen as the result of an event having been sent to the client (either from the activation of a XGrabKey() or from a previous XAllowEvents() with mode SyncKeyboard but not from a XGrabKeyboard()), the keyboard grab is released and that event is completely reprocessed. This time, however, the function ignores any passive grabs at or above (towards the root of) the grab\_window of the grab just released. The request has no effect if the keyboard is not grabbed by the client or if the keyboard is not frozen as the result of an event.
                \item \textbf{SyncBoth}:	If both pointer and keyboard are frozen by the client, event processing for both devices continues as usual until the next ButtonPress, ButtonRelease, KeyPress, or KeyRelease event is reported to the client for a grabbed device (button event for the pointer, key event for the keyboard), at which time the devices again appear to freeze. However, if the reported event causes the grab to be released, then the devices do not freeze (but if the other device is still grabbed, then a subsequent event for it will still cause both devices to freeze). SyncBoth has no effect unless both pointer and keyboard are frozen by the client. If the pointer or keyboard is frozen twice by the client on behalf of two separate grabs, SyncBoth thaws for both (but a subsequent freeze for SyncBoth will only freeze each device once).
                \item \textbf{AsyncBoth}:	If the pointer and the keyboard are frozen by the client, event processing for both devices continues as usual. If a device is frozen twice by the client on behalf of two separate grabs, AsyncBoth thaws for both. AsyncBoth has no effect unless both pointer and keyboard are frozen by the client.
            \end{itemize}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            This is useful when a client wants to inspect an event before deciding whether it should be replayed or swallowed.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            XAllowEvents() can generate a \textbf{BadValue} error.
        \item \textbf{Grab result codes}: A grab can fail, for example
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            int status = XGrabPointer(...);
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Possible result values include:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                GrabSuccess
                AlreadyGrabbed
                GrabInvalidTime
                GrabNotViewable
                GrabFrozen
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            So a grab is not guaranteed. 
        \item \textbf{Save-set}: A save-set is a mechanism used mainly by window managers to avoid destroying or orphaning client windows if the window manager exits. This matters because window managers often reparent client windows.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Normally, an application creates a top-level window as a child of the root window:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \fig{1}{./figures/1.png}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            A reparenting window manager inserts a frame window around it:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \fig{1}{./figures/2.png}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The application window is now a child of a window owned by the window manager. Now, we have a problem...
            \begin{center}
                \textit{If the window manager dies, what happens to the application windows inside its frame windows?}
            \end{center}
            Without protection, those client windows could be destroyed or left in a bad state when the frame windows disappear. So the window manager adds application windows to its save-set:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            XAddToSaveSet(display, client_window)
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            If the window manager exits or its connection to the X server closes, the server automatically reparents those save-set windows, usually back to a safe ancestor such as the root, and maps them if appropriate.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            You can remove a window from the save-set with
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            XRemoveFromSaveSet(display, client_window);
            \end{cppcode}
        \item \textbf{Superior and inferior}: A window's \textbf{inferiors} are its descendants. A window's \textbf{superiors} are its ancestors.
        \item \textbf{Using X Server Connection Close Operations}: When the X server’s connection to a client is closed either by an explicit call to \textbf{XCloseDisplay} or by a process that exits, the X server performs the following automatic operations:
            \begin{itemize}
                \item It disowns all selections owned by the client (see \textbf{XSetSelectionOwner).}
                \item It performs an \textbf{XUngrabPointer} and \textbf{XUngrabKeyboard} if the client has actively grabbed the pointer or the keyboard.
                \item It performs an \textbf{XUngrabServer} if the client has grabbed the server.
                \item It releases all passive grabs made by the client.
                \item It marks all resources (including colormap entries) allocated by the client either as permanent or temporary, depending on whether the close-down mode is \textbf{RetainPermanent} or \textbf{RetainTemporary.} However, this does not prevent other client applications from explicitly destroying the resources (see \textbf{XSetCloseDownMode).}
            \end{itemize}
            When the close-down mode is \textbf{DestroyAll,} the X server destroys all of a client’s resources as follows:
            \begin{itemize}
                \item It examines each window in the client’s save-set to determine if it is an inferior (subwindow) of a window created by the client. (The save-set is a list of other clients’ windows that are referred to as save-set windows.) If so, the X server reparents the save-set window to the closest ancestor so that the save-set window is not an inferior of a window created by the client. The reparenting leaves unchanged the absolute coordinates (with respect to the root window) of the upper-left outer corner of the save-set window.
                \item It performs a \textbf{MapWindow} request on the save-set window if the save-set window is unmapped. The X server does this even if the save-set window was not an inferior of a window created by the client.
                \item It destroys all windows created by the client.
                \item It performs the appropriate free request on each nonwindow resource created by the client in the server (for example, \textbf{Font,} \textbf{Pixmap,} \textbf{Cursor,} \textbf{Colormap,} and \textbf{GContext).}
                \item It frees all colors and colormap entries allocated by a client application
            \end{itemize}
            Additional processing occurs when the last connection to the X server closes. An X server goes through a cycle of having no connections and having some connections. When the last connection to the X server closes as a result of a connection closing with the close\_mode of \textbf{DestroyAll,} the X server does the following:
            \begin{itemize}
                \item It resets its state as if it had just been started. The X server begins by destroying all lingering resources from clients that have terminated in \textbf{RetainPermanent} or \textbf{RetainTemporary} mode.
                \item It deletes all but the predefined atom identifiers.
                \item It deletes all properties on all root windows (see section 4.3).
                \item It resets all device maps and attributes (for example, key click, bell volume, and acceleration) as well as the access control list.
                \item It restores the standard root tiles and cursors.
                \item It restores the default font path.
                \item It restores the input focus to state \textbf{PointerRoot}
            \end{itemize}
            However, the X server does not reset if you close a connection with a close-down mode set to \textbf{RetainPermanent} or \textbf{RetainTemporary}
        \item \textbf{Locking a display}: To lock a display in Xlib means take a mutex-like lock on the \texttt{Display*} so only one thread in your process uses that X connection at a time.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            XLockDisplay(display)
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            While the display is locked, other threads in the same process that try to use the same Display * will block until it is unlocked.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            XUnlockDisplay(display)
            \end{cppcode}
        \item \textbf{Using Xlib with threads}: To initialize support for concurrent threads, use \textbf{XInitThreads}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                Status XInitThreads();
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The \textbf{XInitThreads} function initializes Xlib support for concurrent threads. This function must be the first Xlib function a multi-threaded program calls, and it must complete before any other Xlib call is made. This function returns a nonzero status if initialization was successful; otherwise, it returns zero. On systems that do not support threads, this function always returns zero.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            It is only necessary to call this function if multiple threads might use Xlib concurrently. If all calls to Xlib functions are protected by some other access mechanism (for example, a mutual exclusion lock in a toolkit or through explicit client programming), Xlib thread initialization is not required. It is recommended that single-threaded programs not call this function.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            To lock a display across several Xlib calls, use \textbf{XLockDisplay}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            void XLockDisplay(display)
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The \textbf{XLockDisplay} function locks out all other threads from using the specified display. Other threads attempting to use the display will block until the display is unlocked by this thread. Nested calls to \textbf{XLockDisplay} work correctly; the display will not actually be unlocked until \textbf{XUnlockDisplay} has been called the same number of times as \textbf{XLockDisplay.} This function has no effect unless Xlib was successfully initialized for threads using \textbf{XInitThreads.}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            To unlock a display, use XUnlockDisplay
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            void XUnlockDisplay(display)
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The \textbf{XUnlockDisplay} function allows other threads to use the specified display again. Any threads that have blocked on the display are allowed to continue. Nested locking works correctly; if \textbf{XLockDisplay} has been called multiple times by a thread, then \textbf{XUnlockDisplay} must be called an equal number of times before the display is actually unlocked. This function has no effect unless Xlib was successfully initialized for threads using \textbf{XInitThreads.}
        \item \textbf{Using Internal Connections}: In addition to the connection to the X server, an Xlib implementation may require connections to other kinds of servers. Toolkits and clients that use multiple displays, or that use displays in combination with other inputs, need to obtain these additional connections to correctly block until input is available and need to process that input when it is available. Simple clients that use a single display and block for input in an Xlib event function do not need to use these facilities.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            To track internal connections for a display, use \textbf{XAddConnectionWatch.}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                typedef void (*XConnectionWatchProc) (Display* display, XPointer client_data, int fd, Bool opening, XPointer watch_data)

                Status XAddConnectionWatch (Display* display, XConnectionWatchProc procedure, XPointer client_data)
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item procedure Specifies the procedure to be called.
                \item client\_data Specifies the additional client data.
            \end{itemize}
            The \textbf{XAddConnectionWatch} function registers a procedure to be called each time Xlib opens or closes an internal connection for the specified display. The procedure is passed the display, the specified client\_data, the file descriptor for the connection, a Boolean indicating whether the connection is being opened or closed, and a pointer to a location for private watch data. If opening is \textbf{True,} the procedure can store a pointer to private data in the location pointed to by watch\_data; when the procedure is later called for this same connection and opening is \textbf{False}, the location pointed to by watch\_data will hold this same private data pointer.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            This function can be called at any time after a display is opened. If internal connections already exist, the registered procedure will immediately be called for each of them, before \textbf{XAddConnectionWatch} returns. \textbf{XAddConnectionWatch} returns a nonzero status if the procedure is successfully registered; otherwise, it returns zero
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The registered procedure should not call any Xlib functions. If the procedure directly or indirectly causes the state of internal connections or watch procedures to change, the result is not defined. If Xlib has been initialized for threads, the procedure is called with the display locked and the result of a call by the procedure to any Xlib function that locks the display is not defined unless the executing thread has externally locked the display using \textbf{XLockDisplay}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            To stop tracking internal connections for a display, use \textbf{XRemoveConnectionWatch.}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                Status XRemoveConnectionWatch (display, procedure, client_data)
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The \textbf{XRemoveConnectionWatch} function removes a previously registered connection watch procedure. The client\_data must match the client\_data used when the procedure was initially registered.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            To process input on an internal connection, use \textbf{XProcessInternalConnection}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            void XProcessInternalConnection(Display* display, int fd)
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The \textbf{XProcessInternalConnection} function processes input available on an internal connection. This function should be called for an internal connection only after an operating system facility (for example, select or poll) has indicated that input is available; otherwise, the effect is not defined.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            To obtain all of the current internal connections for a display, use \textbf{XInternalConnectionNumbers.}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                Status XInternalConnectionNumbers(Display* display, int** fd_return, int* count_return)
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The \textbf{XInternalConnectionNumbers} function returns a list of the file descriptors for all internal connections currently open for the specified display. When the allocated list is no longer needed, free it by using \textbf{XFree.} This functions returns a nonzero status if the list is successfully allocated; otherwise, it returns zero.
    \end{itemize}

    \pagebreak 
    \unsect{3. Window functions}
    \begin{itemize}
        \item \textbf{Intro}: In the $X$ Window System, a window is a rectangular area on the screen that lets you view graphic output. Client applications can display overlapping and nested windows on one or more screens that are driven by $X$ servers on one or more machines. Clients who want to create windows must first connect their program to the $X$ server by calling \textbf{XOpenDisplay.} This chapter begins with a discussion of visual types and window attributes. The chapter continues with a discussion of the Xlib functions you can use to:
            \begin{itemize}
                \item Create windows
                \item Destroy windows
                \item Map windows
                \item Unmap windows
                \item Configure windows
                \item Change window stacking order
                \item Change window attributes
            \end{itemize}
        \item \textbf{ICCCM / EWMH conventions}:
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textbf{ICCCM (Inter-client communication conventions)}: The Inter-Client Communication Conventions Manual (ICCCM) is a foundational standard protocol for the X Window System. It establishes the rules that graphical applications (clients) must follow to cooperate, share resources (like cut and paste selections), and interact smoothly with window managers. 
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    The ICCCM focuses on a few core areas of client behavior to ensure a stable and predictable desktop experience:
                    \begin{itemize}
                        \item \textbf{Selections and Cut Buffers}: Defines how clients share data across the global X server, enabling universal copy and paste operations between different applications.
                        \item \textbf{Window Manager Communication}: Establishes the rules for how applications request window sizes, positions, and decorations, ensuring that a window manager can successfully control top-level windows without relying on private protocols.
                        \item \textbf{Session Management}: Provides protocols for applications to interact with session managers, allowing users to safely save their workspace state, restart programs, or shut down without losing functionality.
                        \item \textbf{Shared Resources}: Details the conventions for sharing input focus and color maps, ensuring that apps don't compete or lock each other out of basic system resources.
                    \end{itemize}
                \item \textbf{EWMH (Extended window manager hints)}: EWMH stands for Extended Window Manager Hints. It is a standard for the X Window System that dictates how desktop environments, window managers, and applications communicate with one another. It builds upon older standards to let modern apps request specific behaviors (like going fullscreen or creating custom docks). 
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    The foundational standard for X11 communication (ICCCM) is notoriously complex and lacks the features we expect from modern operating systems, such as virtual desktops, docks, and window tabbing. EWMH bridges this gap by defining properties and messages (called "hints") that allow programs and the window manager to understand each other perfectly. 
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    Under EWMH, the window manager exposes specific pieces of information on the root window, and applications use those to request states or react to desktop events. Key functions include:
                    \begin{itemize}
                        \item \textbf{Window Types \& States}: Apps can declare what they are (e.g., a normal app, a dock, a splash screen, or a utility tool) or request states like \_NET\_WM\_STATE\_FULLSCREEN or \_NET\_WM\_STATE\_MODAL.
                        \item \textbf{Virtual Desktops}: It defines protocols for managing workspaces, including how many virtual screens exist and which desktop a specific window lives on.
                        \item \textbf{Window Tabbing \& Grouping}: It specifies how windows can be logically grouped together (often seen in advanced tiling or tabbed window managers).
                        \item \textbf{Desktop Properties}: It standardizes the retrieval of desktop dimensions, work areas (reserving space for taskbars), and active window tracking. 
                    \end{itemize}
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    Developers working directly with window operations rely on specific EWMH messages (denoted by the \textbf{\_NET} prefix): 
                    \begin{itemize}
                        \item \textbf{\_NET\_ACTIVE\_WINDOW}: Tells the window manager to give focus to a specific window.
                        \item \textbf{\_NET\_CLOSE\_WINDOW}: Requests a graceful closure of an application window.
                        \item \textbf{\_NET\_WM\_PING}: Allows the window manager to check if an application has stopped responding.
                    \end{itemize}
            \end{itemize}
            \textbf{Note:} EWMH is not standalone in the strict compliance sense. The EWMH spec explicitly says it builds on ICCCM, and that window managers and clients that aim to fulfill EWMH must also adhere to ICCCM, except where EWMH explicitly modifies ICCCM behavior.
        \item \textbf{Opaque structures}: An opaque struct is a design pattern in C where a structure is declared but its internal fields are hidden from the user. The user only interacts with the struct via pointers and a provided set of API functions, making it the primary way to achieve encapsulation and object-oriented "data hiding" in C. 
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            A \textbf{transparent (exposed) struct} is the opposite. The fields are fully visible to the user.
        \item \textbf{Visual types}: On some display hardware, it may be possible to deal with color resources in more than one way. For example, you may be able to deal with a screen of either 12-bit depth with arbitrary mapping of pixel to color (pseudo-color) or 24-bit depth with 8 bits of the pixel dedicated to each of red, green, and blue. These different ways of dealing with the visual aspects of the screen are called visuals. For each screen of the display, there may be a list of valid visual types supported at different depths of the screen. Because default windows and visual types are defined for each screen, most simple applications need not deal with this complexity. 
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Xlib uses an opaque Visual structure that contains information about the possible color mapping. The visual utility functions use an \textbf{XVisualInfo} structure to return this information to an application. The members of this structure pertinent to this discussion are class, red\_mask, green\_mask, blue\_mask, bits\_per\_rgb, and colormap\_size. The class member specified one of the possible visual classes of the screen and can be \textbf{StaticGray,} \textbf{StaticColor,} \textbf{TrueColor,} \textbf{GrayScale,} \textbf{PseudoColor,} or \textbf{DirectColor.}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            A pixel value in X11 is usually just an integer. The Visual tells Xlib and the X server what that integer means. For example, does the integer directly encode red/green/blue components? Or is it an index into a colormap? Are the colors fixed, or can the client modify them?
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The \textbf{Visual} describes the color interpretation model available for windows and pixmaps of a certain depth.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                typedef struct {
                    Visual *visual;
                    VisualID visualid;
                    int screen;
                    int depth;
                    int class;
                    unsigned long red_mask;
                    unsigned long green_mask;
                    unsigned long blue_mask;
                    int colormap_size;
                    int bits_per_rgb;
                } XVisualInfo;
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            There is no direct “convert Visual* to XVisualInfo” function, but you can get the VisualID from the Visual *, then ask Xlib for the matching XVisualInfo.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                VisualID XVisualIDFromVisual(Visual* visual);
                XVisualInfo* XGetVisualInfo(
                    Display* display,
                    long vinfo_mask,
                    XVisualInfo* vinfo_template,
                    int* nitems_return
                );
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item vinfo\_mask	Specifies the visual mask value.
                \item vinfo\_template	Specifies the visual attributes that are to be used in matching the visual structures.
                \item nitems\_return	Returns the number of matching visual structures.
            \end{itemize}
            Regarding the vinfo\_mask: In Xlib, vinfo\_mask is a bitwise mask value used as an argument in the XGetVisualInfo function to specify which fields of an XVisualInfo template structure the X server should evaluate when searching for available window visuals. When you use XGetVisualInfo, you pass a template structure containing the display criteria you want. The vinfo\_mask tells the function exactly which of those template fields matter for your query
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textbf{VisualNoMask}: No fields are matched (returns all available visuals)
                \item \textbf{VisualIDMask}: Matches the explicit visualid 
                \item \textbf{IDVisualScreenMask}: Matches the specified screen 
                \item \textbf{numberVisualDepthMask}: Matches the pixel bit depth (e.g., 24-bit, 32-bit color)
                \item \textbf{VisualClassMask}: Matches the color class (e.g., TrueColor, PseudoColor)
                \item \textbf{VisualRedMaskMask}: Matches the red\_mask bit 
                \item \textbf{positionsVisualGreenMaskMask}: Matches the green\_mask bit 
                \item \textbf{positionsVisualBlueMaskMask}: Matches the blue\_mask bit 
                \item \textbf{positionsVisualColormapSizeMask}: Matches the total entries in the colormap\_size
                \item \textbf{VisualBitsPerRGBMask}: Matches the bits\_per\_rgb value
                \item \textbf{VisualAllMask}: Combines all masks to check every field in the structure
            \end{itemize}
            For example,
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                XVisualInfo template;
                template.screen = screen;
                template.depth = 32;
                template.class = TrueColor;

                int nitems;
                XVisualInfo *matches = XGetVisualInfo(
                    dpy,
                    VisualScreenMask | VisualDepthMask | VisualClassMask,
                    &template,
                    &nitems
                );
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textbf{Class}: The class field tells you what kind of color model the visual uses. There are six visual classes
                    \begin{enumerate}
                        \item \textbf{StaticGray}: A StaticGray visual represents fixed grayscale colors. The pixel value selects a gray value from a read-only colormap.
                            \bigbreak \noindent 
                            You cannot change what gray value a pixel maps to. This is mostly relevant to old or limited displays.
                        \item \textbf{GrayScale}: A GrayScale visual is like StaticGray, except the colormap entries can be changed. The colors are grayscale, but the client may allocate or change entries.
                        \item \textbf{StaticColor}: A StaticColor visual uses a fixed color colormap. The pixel value indexes into a predefined table of colors. The client cannot redefine those colors.
                        \item \textbf{PseudoColor}: A PseudoColor visual uses a modifiable colormap. For example, pixel value 42 does not inherently mean red or blue. It means “look at entry 42 in the colormap.”
                            \bigbreak \noindent 
                            The client may allocate/change colormap entries, depending on availability. This was common on older 8-bit indexed-color displays.
                        \item \textbf{TrueColor}: A TrueColor visual means the pixel value directly contains RGB components. For example, with a common 24-bit / 32-bit visual.
                            \begin{center}
                                [pixel bits]: [[red bits] [green bits] [blue bits]]
                            \end{center}
                            The masks say where the components are stored. For example,
                            \bigbreak \noindent 
                            \begin{cppcode}
                                red_mask   = 0x00ff0000
                                green_mask = 0x0000ff00
                                blue_mask  = 0x000000ff
                            \end{cppcode}
                            \bigbreak \noindent 
                            So a pixel value like
                            \begin{align*}
                                0x00ff0000
                            .\end{align*}
                            means full red, zero green, zero blue.
                            \bigbreak \noindent 
                            In TrueColor, the mapping from component value to actual intensity is fixed. You cannot redefine what “red component 255” means. This is the usual modern visual class.
                        \item \textbf{DirectColor}: DirectColor is similar to TrueColor because the pixel value contains separate red, green, and blue fields.
                            \bigbreak \noindent 
                            But instead of those fields directly giving final intensities, each component indexes into a separate modifiable lookup table.
                    \end{enumerate}
                    The main distinction is 
                    \begin{itemize}
                        \item \textbf{Static* classes}: colormap entries are fixed/read-only
                        \item \textbf{Non-static classes}: colormap entries can be changed by the client
                        \item \textbf{TrueColor / DirectColor}: pixel value contains separate RGB component fields
                        \item \textbf{PseudoColor / StaticColor / GrayScale / StaticGray}: pixel value indexes into a colormap
                    \end{itemize}
                \item \textbf{red\_mask, green\_mask, blue\_mask}: Described above in the \textit{class} explanation.
                \item \textbf{bits\_per\_rgb}: Tells you the number of significant bits used for each RGB value in the colormap. For example, if
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    \begin{cppcode}
                    bits_per_rgb = 8;
                    \end{cppcode}
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    then each color component has 8 meaningful bits, giving values from 0 to 255.
                \item \textbf{colormap\_size}: The number of entries available in the colormap for that visual.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    For example, an 8-bit PseudoColor visual might have:
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    \begin{cppcode}
                    colormap_size = 256; 
                    \end{cppcode}
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    because 8-bit pixels can index 256 possible entries.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    For TrueColor, the meaning is slightly different. It usually refers to the number of entries per primary color map, not the total number of possible RGB colors.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    So for a 24-bit TrueColor visual with 8 bits per channel, you might see:
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    \begin{cppcode}
                        colormap_size = 256; 
                    \end{cppcode}
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    Even though the visual can represent approximately
                    \begin{align*}
                        256^{3} = 16777216
                    \end{align*}
                    colors.
            \end{itemize}
            \textbf{Note:} An X11 client cannot define a custom \texttt{Visual}. A Visual is advertised by the X server as part of the screen’s capabilities. When your program connects to the X server, the server tells the client which visuals exist for each screen. Your program can then choose from those visuals, but it cannot create a new one.
        \item \textbf{Top-level windows}: A top-level window is any window that is a direct subwindow of your screen's root window and is independently managed by your desktop's window manager.
        \item \textbf{Window attributes}: All \textbf{InputOutput} windows have a border width of zero or more pixels, an optional background, an event suppression mask (which suppresses propagation of events from children), and a property list. The window border and background can be a solid color or a pattern, called a tile. All windows except the root have a parent and are clipped by their parent. If a window is stacked on top of another window, it obscures that other window for the purpose of input.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            If a window has a background (almost all do), it obscures the other window for purposes of output. Attempts to output to the obscured area do nothing, and no input events (for example, pointer motion) are generated for the obscured area.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Both \textbf{InputOutput} and \textbf{InputOnly} windows have the following common attributes, which are the only attributes of an \textbf{InputOnly} window:
            \begin{itemize}
                \item win-gravity
                \item event-mask
                \item do-not-propagate-mask
                \item override-redirect
                \item cursor
            \end{itemize}
            If you specify any other attributes for an \textbf{InputOnly} window, a \textbf{BadMatch} error results.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \textbf{InputOnly} windows are used for controlling input events in situations where \textbf{InputOutput} windows are unnecessary. \textbf{InputOnly} windows are invisible; can only be used to control such things as cursors, input event generation, and grabbing; and cannot be used in any graphics requests. Note that \textbf{InputOnly} windows cannot have \textbf{InputOutput} windows as inferiors
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Windows have borders of a programmable width and pattern as well as a background pattern or tile. Pixel values can be used for solid colors. The background and border pixmaps can be destroyed immediately after creating the window if no further explicit references to them are to be made. The pattern can either be relative to the parent or absolute. If \textbf{ParentRelative,} the parent’s background is used.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            When windows are first created, they are not visible (not mapped) on the screen. Any output to a window that is not visible on the screen and that does not have backing store will be discarded. An application may wish to create a window long before it is mapped to the screen. When a window is eventually mapped to the screen (using \textbf{XMapWindow),} the X server generates an Expose event for the window if backing store has not been maintained.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            A window manager can override your choice of size, border width, and position for a top-level window. Your program must be prepared to use the actual size and position of the top window. It is not acceptable for a client application to resize itself unless in direct response to a human command to do so. Instead, either your program should use the space given to it, or if the space is too small for any useful work, your program might ask the user to resize the window. The border of your top-level window is considered fair game for window managers.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            To set an attribute of a window, set the appropriate member of the \textbf{XSetWindowAttributes} structure and OR in the corresponding value bitmask in your subsequent calls to \textbf{XCreateWindow} and \textbf{XChangeWindowAttributes,} or use one of the other convenience functions that set the appropriate attribute. The symbols for the value mask bits and the \textbf{XSetWindowAttributes} structure are:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                /* Window attribute value mask bits */
                #define CWBackPixmap        (1L<<0)
                #define CWBackPixel         (1L<<1)
                #define CWBorderPixmap      (1L<<2)
                #define CWBorderPixel       (1L<<3)
                #define CWBitGravity        (1L<<4)
                #define CWWinGravity        (1L<<5)
                #define CWBackingStore      (1L<<6)
                #define CWBackingPlanes     (1L<<7)
                #define CWBackingPixel      (1L<<8)
                #define CWOverrideRedirect  (1L<<9)
                #define CWSaveUnder         (1L<<10)
                #define CWEventMask         (1L<<11)
                #define CWDontPropagate     (1L<<12)
                #define CWColormap          (1L<<13)
                #define CWCursor            (1L<<14)
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                typedef struct {
                    Pixmap background_pixmap;       /* background, None, or ParentRelative */
                    unsigned long background_pixel; /* background pixel */
                    Pixmap border_pixmap;           /* border of the window or CopyFromParent */
                    unsigned long border_pixel;     /* border pixel value */
                    int bit_gravity;                /* one of bit gravity values */
                    int win_gravity;                /* one of the window gravity values */
                    int backing_store;              /* NotUseful, WhenMapped, Always */
                    unsigned long backing_planes;   /* planes to be preserved if possible */
                    unsigned long backing_pixel;    /* value to use in restoring planes */
                    Bool save_under;                /* should bits under be saved? (popups) */
                    long event_mask;                /* set of events that should be saved */
                    long do_not_propagate_mask;     /* set of events that should not propagate */
                    Bool override_redirect;         /* boolean value for override_redirect */
                    Colormap colormap;              /* color map to be associated with window */
                    Cursor cursor;                  /* cursor to be displayed (or None) */
                } XSetWindowAttributes;
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textbf{background\_pixmap}: Background\_pixmap is a pixmap resource used as a tiled background. It can be:
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    \begin{cppcode}
                        None
                        ParentRelative
                        some_pixmap
                    \end{cppcode}
                    \begin{itemize}
                        \item \textbf{None:} None means the window has no automatic background. X will not repaint the background for you.
                        \item \textbf{ParentRelative}: ParentRelative means the window uses the parent window’s background. This is only valid for windows with the same depth as the parent.
                        \item \textbf{real pixmap}: A real pixmap means the server tiles that pixmap across the window background.
                    \end{itemize}
                \item \textbf{long background\_pixel}: background\_pixel is simpler: it fills the background with one pixel value. The meaning of that pixel value depends on the window’s colormap and visual. In a TrueColor visual, it usually encodes RGB bits. In PseudoColor, it is an index into a colormap.
                \item \textbf{border\_pixmap}: border\_pixmap uses a pixmap as the border pattern. It can also be CopyFromParent in some creation contexts.
                \item \textbf{long border\_pixel}: border\_pixel uses a single pixel value for the border color.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    The border is the X window border specified by the border\_width argument to XCreateWindow. Many modern window managers ignore or replace client-side borders for top-level windows because they draw their own decorations.
                \item \textbf{bit\_gravity}: Bit gravity controls what happens to the contents of the window when the window is resized.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    For example, suppose your window gets larger. Should the old pixels stay in the upper-left corner? Center? Bottom-right? Or should X discard them?
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    Common values include
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    \begin{cppcode}
                        ForgetGravity
                        NorthWestGravity
                        NorthGravity
                        NorthEastGravity
                        WestGravity
                        CenterGravity
                        EastGravity
                        SouthWestGravity
                        SouthGravity
                        SouthEastGravity
                        StaticGravity
                    \end{cppcode}
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    So,
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    \begin{cppcode}
                    attrs.bit_gravity = NorthWestGravity;
                    \end{cppcode}
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    This means that when the window is resized, X tries to keep the old pixel contents attached to the upper-left corner.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    \textbf{ForgetGravity} means X does not preserve the old bits. You should repaint when you receive an Expose event.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    In modern applications, you usually repaint explicitly instead of relying heavily on bit gravity.
                \item \textbf{win\_gravity}: Window gravity controls what happens to a child window’s position when its parent is resized.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    \begin{cppcode}
                    attrs.win_gravity = SouthEastGravity;
                    \end{cppcode}
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    means that when the parent resizes, $X$ tries to keep the child attached to the parent’s bottom-right region.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    This matters mostly for child-window layout. It is not usually used as the primary layout mechanism in modern GUI toolkits.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    A win-gravity of \textbf{UnmapGravity} is like \textbf{NorthWestGravity} (the window is not moved), except the child is also unmapped when the parent is resized, and an \textbf{UnmapNotify} event is generated.
                \item \textbf{backing\_store}:  Backing store asks the $X$ server to preserve obscured or unmapped window contents, so that when the window becomes visible again, the server may restore pixels without requiring the client to repaint.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    Possible values are
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    \begin{cppcode}
                        NotUseful
                        WhenMapped
                        Always
                    \end{cppcode}
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    \begin{itemize}
                        \item NotUseful means you do not request backing store.
                        \item WhenMapped means the server may preserve contents while the window is mapped.
                        \item Always means the server should try to preserve contents more aggressively.
                    \end{itemize}
                    However, this is only a hint. The server is not required to honor it. Modern systems often ignore backing store or implement it differently because compositors already keep window buffers.
                \item \textbf{backing\_planes}: backing\_planes specifies which bit planes should be preserved.
                \item \textbf{backing\_pixel}: backing\_pixel gives a fallback pixel value for restoring planes that were not preserved.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    Most modern Xlib programs simply handle Expose events and repaint instead of relying on backing store.
                \item \textbf{save\_under}: save\_under is a hint to the server that it should save the pixels underneath this window while it is visible.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    This was intended for temporary popup windows, menus, tooltips, and similar small transient windows.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    Like backing store, this is only a hint. The server may ignore it.
                \item \textbf{event\_mask}: This selects which events your client wants to receive for this window.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    Some common masks are
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    \begin{cppcode}
                        ExposureMask
                        KeyPressMask
                        KeyReleaseMask
                        ButtonPressMask
                        ButtonReleaseMask
                        PointerMotionMask
                        EnterWindowMask
                        LeaveWindowMask
                        StructureNotifyMask
                        SubstructureNotifyMask
                        SubstructureRedirectMask
                        PropertyChangeMask
                        FocusChangeMask
                    \end{cppcode}
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    For example, if you do not select \textbf{KeyPressMask,} then your client will not receive \textbf{KeyPress} events for that window.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    Some event masks are exclusive. For example, only one client can select \textbf{SubstructureRedirectMask} on a given window. That is how a window manager claims control over the root window’s child-window management.
                \item \textbf{do\_not\_propagate\_mask}: This controls which input events should not propagate upward to ancestor windows.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    In $x$, some events can propagate from a child window to its parent if no client selected them on the child.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    For example, suppose a button press occurs in a child window. If nobody selected \textbf{ButtonPressMask} on that child, the event may propagate to the parent.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    The \textbf{do\_not\_propagate\_mask} can stop that propagation.
                \item \textbf{override\_redirect}: This tells the X server whether the window manager should be bypassed for this window. If true, then the window manager normally does not manage the window. That means no decorations, no placement policy, no focus policy, no normal reparenting, and no window-manager intervention.
                \item \textbf{colormap}: This is the colormap associated with the window.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    A colormap maps pixel values to actual colors, depending on the visual class.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    For common TrueColor visuals, you usually use the default colormap:
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    But if you create a window using a non-default visual, you often need to create and assign a matching colormap:
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    \begin{cppcode}
                        Colormap cmap = XCreateColormap(display, root, visual, AllocNone);
                        attrs.colormap = cmap;
                    \end{cppcode}
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    This is especially important when creating windows with 32-bit ARGB visuals for transparency. The window’s visual and colormap must be compatible.
                \item \textbf{cursor}: This specifies the cursor shown when the pointer is inside the window.
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    You can use a cursor created with functions like:
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    \begin{cppcode}
                        XCreateFontCursor
                        XCreatePixmapCursor
                        XcursorLibraryLoadCursor
                    \end{cppcode}
                    \bigbreak \noindent 
                    If the cursor is None, the cursor is inherited from the parent window.
            \end{itemize}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The following lists the defaults for each window attribute and indicates whether the attribute is applicable to \textbf{InputOutput} and \textbf{InputOnly} windows:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{center}
                \begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{@{}XXll@{}}
                    \toprule
                    \textbf{Attribute}& \textbf{Default}& \textbf{InputOutput}& \textbf{InputOnly} \\
                    \midrule
                    background-pixmap &None &Yes &No\\[2ex]
                    background-pixel &Undefined &Yes &No\\[2ex]
                    border-pixmap &CopyFromParent &Yes &No\\[2ex]
                    border-pixel &Undefined &Yes &No\\[2ex]
                    bit-gravity &ForgetGravity &Yes &No\\[2ex]
                    win-gravity &NorthWestGravity &Yes &Yes\\[2ex]
                    backing-store &NotUseful &Yes &No\\[2ex]
                    backing-planes& All ones &Yes &No\\[2ex]
                    backing-pixel &zero &Yes &No \\[2ex]
                    save-under &False &Yes &No\\[2ex]
                    event-mask &empty set &Yes &Yes\\[2ex]
                    do-not-propagate-mask &empty set &Yes &Yes\\[2ex]
                    override-redirect &False &Yes &Yes\\[2ex]
                    colormap &CopyFromParent &Yes &No\\[2ex]
                    cursor &None &Yes &Yes \\
                    \bottomrule
                \end{tabularx}
            \end{center}

        \item \textbf{Background Attribute}: Only \textbf{InputOutput} windows can have a background. You can set the background of an \textbf{InputOutput} window by using a pixel or a pixmap.
        \item \textbf{Pixel centers}: In graphics, to \textbf{coincide with pixel centers} means that some geometric coordinate, such as a line, point, or sample location, falls exactly at the center of a pixel, not on the boundary between pixels.
        \item \textbf{Creating Windows}: Xlib provides basic ways for creating windows, and toolkits often supply higher-level functions specifically for creating and placing top-level windows, which are discussed in the appropriate toolkit documentation. If you do not use a toolkit, however, you must provide some standard information or hints for the window manager by using the Xlib inter-client communication functions 
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            If you use Xlib to create your own top-level windows (direct children of the root window), you must observe the following rules so that all applications interact reasonably across the different styles of window management:
            \begin{itemize}
                \item You must never fight with the window manager for the size or placement of your top-level window.
                \item You must be able to deal with whatever size window you get, even if this means that your application just prints a message like ‘‘Please make me bigger’’ in its window.
                \item You should only attempt to resize or move top-level windows in direct response to a user request. If a request to change the size of a top-level window fails, you must be prepared to live with what you get. You are free to resize or move the children of top-level windows as necessary. (Toolkits often have facilities for automatic relayout.)
                \item If you do not use a toolkit that automatically sets standard window properties, you should set these properties for top-level windows before mapping them.
            \end{itemize}
            \textbf{XCreateWindow} is the more general function that allows you to set specific window attributes when you create a window. \textbf{XCreateSimpleWindow} creates a window that inherits its attributes from its parent window.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The X server acts as if \textbf{InputOnly} windows do not exist for the purposes of graphics requests, exposure processing, and \textbf{VisibilityNotify} events. An \textbf{InputOnly} window cannot be used as a drawable (that is, as a source or destination for graphics requests). \textbf{InputOnly} and \textbf{InputOutput} windows act identically in other respects (properties, grabs, input control, and so on). Extension packages can define other classes of windows.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            To create an unmapped window and set its window attributes, use \textbf{XCreateWindow}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                Window XCreateWindow(
                    Display* display, 
                    Window parent,
                    int x, y, 
                    unsigned int width, height, 
                    unsigned int border_width,
                    int depth,
                    unsigned int class,
                    Visual* visual,
                    unsigned long valuemask,
                    XSetWindowAttributes* attributes
                )
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textbf{display}: Specifies the connection to the X server.
                \item \textbf{parent}: Specifies the parent window.
                \item \textbf{$\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y}$}: Specify the $x$ and $y$ coordinates, which are the top-left outside corner of the created window’s borders and are relative to the inside of the parent window’s borders.
                \item \textbf{width, height}: Specify the width and height, which are the created window’s inside dimensions and do not include the created window’s borders. The dimensions must be nonzero, or a \textbf{BadValue} error results.
                \item \textbf{border\_width}: Specifies the width of the created window’s border in pixels.
                \item \textbf{depth}: Specifies the window’s depth. A depth of \textbf{CopyFromParent} means the depth is taken from the parent.
                \item \textbf{class}: Specifies the created window’s class. You can pass \textbf{InputOutput,} \textbf{InputOnly,} or \textbf{CopyFromParent.} A class of \textbf{CopyFromParent} means the class is taken from the parent.
                \item \textbf{visual}: Specifies the visual type. A visual of \textbf{CopyFromParent} means the visual type is taken from the parent.
                \item \textbf{valuemask}: Specifies which window attributes are defined in the attributes argument. This mask is the bitwise inclusive OR of the valid attribute mask bits. If valuemask is zero, the attributes are ignored and are not referenced.
                \item \textbf{attributes}: Specifies the structure from which the values (as specified by the value mask) are to be taken. The value mask should have the appropriate bits set to indicate which attributes have been set in the structure.
            \end{itemize}
            The \textbf{XCreateWindow} function creates an unmapped subwindow for a specified parent window, returns the window ID of the created window, and causes the X server to generate a \textbf{CreateNotify} event. The created window is placed on top in the stacking order with respect to siblings.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The coordinate system has the $X$ axis horizontal and the $Y$ axis vertical with the origin [0, 0] at the upper-left corner. Coordinates are integral, in terms of pixels, and coincide with pixel centers. Each window and pixmap has its own coordinate system. For a window, the origin is inside the border at the inside, upper-left corner.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The border\_width for an \textbf{InputOnly} window must be zero, or a \textbf{BadMatch} error results. For class \textbf{InputOutput,} the visual type and depth must be a combination supported for the screen, or a \textbf{BadMatch} error results. The depth need not be the same as the parent, but the parent must not be a window of class \textbf{InputOnly,} or a \textbf{BadMatch} error results. For an \textbf{InputOnly} window, the depth must be zero, and the visual must be one supported by the screen. If either condition is not met, a \textbf{BadMatch} error results. The parent window, however, may have any depth and class. If you specify any invalid window attribute for a window, a \textbf{BadMatch} error results.
        \item \textbf{Creating simple windows}: The created window is not yet displayed (mapped) on the user’s display. To display the window, call \textbf{XMapWindow.} The new window initially uses the same cursor as its parent. A new cursor can be defined for the new window by calling \textbf{XDefineCursor.} The window will not be visible on the screen unless it and all of its ancestors are mapped and it is not obscured by any of its ancestors.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            XCreateWindow can generate \textbf{BadAlloc,} \textbf{BadColor,} \textbf{BadCursor,} \textbf{BadMatch,} \textbf{BadPixmap,} \textbf{BadValue,} and \textbf{BadWindow} errors.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            To create an unmapped \textbf{InputOutput} subwindow of a given parent window, use \textbf{XCreateSimpleWindow}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                Window XCreateSimpleWindow(
                    Display* display,
                    Window parent, 
                    int x, y,
                    unsigned int width, height,
                    unsigned int border_width,
                    unsigned long border,
                    unsigned long background
                )
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textbf{display}: Specifies the connection to the $X$ server.
                \item \textbf{parent}: Specifies the parent window.
                \item \textbf{$x$, y}: Specify the $x$ and $y$ coordinates, which are the top-left outside corner of the new window’s borders and are relative to the inside of the parent window’s borders.
                \item \textbf{width, height}: Specify the width and height, which are the created window’s inside dimensions and do not include the created window’s borders. The dimensions must be nonzero, or a \textbf{BadValue} error results.
                \item \textbf{border\_width}: Specifies the width of the created window’s border in pixels.
                \item \textbf{border}: Specifies the border pixel value of the window.
                \item \textbf{background}: Specifies the background pixel value of the window.
            \end{itemize}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The \textbf{XCreateSimpleWindow} function creates an unmapped \textbf{InputOutput} subwindow for a specified parent window, returns the window ID of the created window, and causes the X server to generate a \textbf{CreateNotify} event. The created window is placed on top in the stacking order with respect to siblings. Any part of the window that extends outside its parent window is clipped. The border\_width for an \textbf{InputOnly} window must be zero, or a \textbf{BadMatch} error results. \textbf{XCreateSimpleWindow} inherits its depth, class, and visual from its parent. All other window attributes, except background and border, have their default values.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \textbf{XCreateSimpleWindow} can generate \textbf{BadAlloc,} \textbf{BadMatch,} \textbf{BadValue,} and \textbf{BadWindow} errors.
        \item \textbf{Destroying Windows}: Xlib provides functions that you can use to destroy a window or destroy all subwindows of a window.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            To destroy a window and all of its subwindows, use \textbf{XDestroyWindow.}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            XDestroyWindow(Display* display, Window w)
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The \textbf{XDestroyWindow} function destroys the specified window as well as all of its subwindows and causes the X server to generate a \textbf{DestroyNotify} event for each window. The window should never be referenced again. If the window specified by the $w$ argument is mapped, it is unmapped automatically. The ordering of the \textbf{DestroyNotify} events is such that for any given window being destroyed, \textbf{DestroyNotify} is generated on any inferiors of the window before being generated on the window itself. The ordering among siblings and across \textbf{subhierarchies} is not otherwise constrained. If the window you specified is a root window, no windows are destroyed. Destroying a mapped window will generate Expose events on other windows that were obscured by the window being destroyed.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \textbf{XDestroyWindow} can generate a \textbf{BadWindow} error
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            To destroy all subwindows of a specified window, use \textbf{XDestroySubwindows}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            XDestroySubwindows(Display* display, Window w) 
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The \textbf{XDestroySubwindows} function destroys all inferior windows of the \textbf{speciﬁed} window, in bottom-to-top stacking order. It causes the X server to generate a \textbf{DestroyNotify} event for each window. If any mapped subwindows were actually destroyed, \textbf{XDestroySubwindows} causes the X server to generate \textbf{Expose} events on the specified window. This is much more efficient than deleting many windows one at a time because much of the work need be performed only once for all of the windows, rather than for each window. The subwindows should never be referenced again.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \textbf{XDestroySubwindows} can generate a \textbf{BadWindow} error.
        \item \textbf{Window stacking order}: The window stacking order is the front-to-back ordering of sibling windows on the screen.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            A window can have many child windows. Among those children, X keeps an ordering that determines which windows appear above or below the others when they overlap.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Think of it like a stack of sheets of paper:
            \begin{align*}
                &\text{Topmost window} \\
                &\uparrow \\
                &\mid \\
                &\text{Window $C$} \\
                &\text{Window $B$} \\
                &\text{Window $A$} \\
                &\mid \\
                &\downarrow \\
                &\text{Bottommost window}
            .\end{align*}
            If Window $C$ overlaps Window $B$, and Window $C$ is higher in the stacking order, then Window C visually covers that part of Window $B$.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The important point is that stacking order is defined relative to windows with the same parent.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{verbatim}
                Root window
                    |-- A
                    |-- B
                    |-- C
            \end{verbatim}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Here, $A$, $B$, and $C$ are siblings. Their stacking order determines which one appears above the others when they overlap.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            But, if $A$ has children:
            \begin{verbatim}
                Root window
                 |--A
                 |  |--A1
                 |  |--A2
                 |--B
                 |--C
            \end{verbatim}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Then $A_{1}$ and $A_{2}$ have their own stacking order relative to each other. They are not directly stacked against $B$ and $C$; they are inside the subtree of $A$.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            If two sibling windows overlap, the one higher in the stack obscures the lower one.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Top-level application windows are usually children of the root window. So the stacking order of top-level windows is the stacking order of the root window’s children.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            A window manager manipulates this order constantly. When you focus a window, open a menu, raise a dialog, or move a window to the front, the window manager changes the stacking order.
        \item \textbf{Mapping windows}: A window is considered mapped if an \textbf{XMapWindow} call has been made on it. It may not be visible on the screen for one of the following reasons:
            \begin{itemize}
                \item It is obscured by another opaque window.
                \item One of its ancestors is not mapped.
                \item It is entirely clipped by an ancestor.
            \end{itemize}
            Expose events are generated for the window when part or all of it becomes visible on the screen. A client receives the Expose events only if it has asked for them. Windows retain their position in the stacking order when they are unmapped.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            A window manager may want to control the placement of subwindows. If \textbf{SubstructureRedirectMask} has been selected by a window manager on a parent window (usually a root window), a map request initiated by other clients on a child window is not performed, and the window manager is sent a \textbf{MapRequest} event. However, if the override-redirect flag on the child had been set to True (usually only on pop-up menus), the map request is performed.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            A tiling window manager might decide to reposition and resize other clients’ windows and then decide to map the window to its final location. A window manager that wants to provide decoration might reparent the child into a frame first. Only a single client at a time can select for \textbf{SubstructureRedirectMask.}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Similarly, a single client can select for \textbf{ResizeRedirectMask} on a parent window. Then, any attempt to resize the window by another client is suppressed, and the client receives a \textbf{ResizeRequest} event.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            To map a given window, use \textbf{XMapWindow}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            XMapWindow(display, w)
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The \textbf{XMapWindow} function maps the window and all of its subwindows that have had map requests. Mapping a window that has an unmapped ancestor does not display the window but marks it as eligible for display when the ancestor becomes mapped. Such a window is called \textbf{unviewable.} When all its ancestors are mapped, the window becomes viewable and will be visible on the screen if it is not obscured by another window. This function has no effect if the window is already mapped.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            If the override-redirect of the window is \textbf{False} and if some other client has selected \textbf{SubstructureRedirectMask} on the parent window, then the X server generates a \textbf{MapRequest} event, and the \textbf{XMapWindow} function does not map the window. Otherwise, the window is mapped, and the X server generates a \textbf{MapNotify} event.
