A selection is global in nature (considered to belong to the user but be maintained by clients) rather than being private to a particular window subhierarchy or a particular set of clients. Xlib provides functions that you can use to set, get, or request conversion of selections. This allows applications to implement the notion of current selection, which requires that notification
        be sent to applications when they no longer own the selection. Applications that support selection often highlight the current selection and so must be informed when another application has acquired the selection so that they can unhighlight the selection.
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        When a client asks for the contents of a selection, it specifies a selection target type. This target type can be used to control the transmitted representation of the contents. For example, if the selection is ‘‘the last thing the user clicked on’’ and that is currently an image, then the target type might specify whether the contents of the image should be sent in XY format or Z format.
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        The target type can also be used to control the class of contents transmitted, for example, asking for the ‘‘looks’’ (fonts, line spacing, indentation, and so forth) of a paragraph selection, not the text of the paragraph. The target type can also be used for other purposes. The protocol does not constrain the semantics.
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        A normal X property is stored on a window:
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        \begin{cppcode}
        window W
          property WM_NAME = "Terminal"
          type STRING
          format 8
        \end{cppcode}
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        The X server stores that property value. Another client can ask the server for WM\_NAME, and the server can directly return the data.
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        A selection is different. For example, with the clipboard:
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        \begin{cppcode}
            selection CLIPBOARD
                owner = some client window
        \end{cppcode}
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        The X server usually does not store the clipboard contents itself. It only knows which client currently owns the selection. The actual data is maintained by the owner client. That is why it is called an \textbf{indirect property:} you do not directly read the value from the server; you ask the server who owns it, and the owner client provides the value. Xlib describes this as a selection being maintained by a client rather than stored in the X server.
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        The \textbf{dynamic type} part means the requester can ask for the same selection in different forms.
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        For example, suppose an application owns the \textbf{CLIPBOARD} selection and the selected content is rich text. Another client might request it as:
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        \begin{cppcode}
            UTF8_STRING      // plain UTF-8 text
            STRING           // older Latin-1-ish text
            text/html        // HTML representation
            image/png        // PNG representation, if applicable
            TARGETS          // list of supported conversion targets
        \end{cppcode}
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        So the “type” is not fixed ahead of time like an ordinary property. The requester specifies a \textbf{target type}, and the owner decides whether it can convert the selection into that representation. Xlib says that when a client asks for a selection, it specifies a target type, which controls the transmitted representation.
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        To set the selection owner, use XSetSelectionOwner.
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        \begin{cppcode}
            XSetSelectionOwner(Display* display, Atom selection, Window owner, Time time)
        \end{cppcode}
        \begin{itemize}
            \item display Specifies the connection to the X server.
            \item selection Specifies the selection atom.
            \item owner Specifies the owner of the specified selection atom. You can pass a window or \textbf{None.}
            \item time Specifies the time. You can pass either a timestamp or \textbf{CurrentTime.}
        \end{itemize}
        The \textbf{XSetSelectionOwner} function changes the owner and last-change time for the specified selection and has no effect if the specified time is earlier than the current last-change time of the specified selection or is later than the current X server time. Otherwise, the last-change time is set to the specified time, with \textbf{CurrentTime} replaced by the current server time. If the owner window is specified as \textbf{None,} then the owner of the selection becomes \textbf{None} (that is, no owner). Otherwise, the owner of the selection becomes the client executing the request.
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        If the new owner (whether a client or None) is not the same as the current owner of the selection and the current owner is not None, the current owner is sent a \textbf{SelectionClear} event. If the client that is the owner of a selection is later terminated (that is, its connection is closed) or if the owner window it has specified in the request is later destroyed, the owner of the selection automatically reverts to \textbf{None,} but the last-change time is not affected. The selection atom is uninterpreted by the X server. \textbf{XGetSelectionOwner} returns the owner window, which is reported in \textbf{SelectionRequest} and \textbf{SelectionClear} events. Selections are global to the X server.
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        \textbf{XSetSelectionOwner} can generate \textbf{BadAtom} and \textbf{BadWindow} errors.
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        The current owner does not get to veto another client taking ownership. the X server changes the owner of that selection, assuming the timestamp is valid. If the new owner is different from the old owner, the old owner is simply sent a SelectionClear event telling it that it lost ownership. The old owner is not asked for permission.
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        To return the selection owner, use \textbf{XGetSelectionOwner.}
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        \begin{cppcode}
            Window XGetSelectionOwner (Display* display, Atom selection)
        \end{cppcode}
        \begin{itemize}
            \item display Specifies the connection to the X server.
            \item selection Specifies the selection atom whose owner you want returned.
        \end{itemize}
        The \textbf{XGetSelectionOwner} function returns the window ID associated with the window that currently owns the specified selection. If no selection was specified, the function returns the constant None. If None is returned, there is no owner for the selection.
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        \textbf{XGetSelectionOwner} can generate a \textbf{BadAtom} error.
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        To request conversion of a selection, use \textbf{XConvertSelection.}
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        \begin{cppcode}
            XConvertSelection (
                Display* display,
                Atom selection,
                Atom target,
                Atom property,
                Window requestor,
                Time time
            )
        \end{cppcode}
        \begin{itemize}
            \item display Specifies the connection to the X server.
            \item selection Specifies the selection atom.
            \item target Specifies the target atom.
            \item property Specifies the property name. You also can pass None.
            \item requestor Specifies the requestor.
            \item time Specifies the time. You can pass either a timestamp or CurrentTime.
        \end{itemize}
        \textbf{XConvertSelection} requests that the specified selection be converted to the specified target type:
        \begin{itemize}
            \item If the specified selection has an owner, the X server sends a \textbf{SelectionRequest} event to that owner.
            \item If no owner for the specified selection exists, the X server generates a \textbf{SelectionNotify} event to the requestor with property \textbf{None.}
        \end{itemize}
        The arguments are passed on unchanged in either of the events. There are two predefined selection atoms: \textbf{PRIMARY} and \textbf{SECONDARY.}
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        \textbf{XConvertSelection} can generate \textbf{BadAtom} and \textbf{BadWindow} errors.
        \bigbreak \noindent 
        The owner of the selection is responsible for deciding what data to put into the requestor’s property. The flow is
        \begin{enumerate}
            \item requestor calls \textbf{XConvertSelection}
            \item X server sends \textbf{SelectionRequest} to the owner
            \item owner examines the request
            \item owner writes data into a property on the \textbf{requestor's} window
            \item owner sends \textbf{SelectionNotify} back to the requestor
            \item requestor reads the property with \textbf{XGetWindowProperty}
        \end{enumerate}






        



    \end{itemize}

    \pagebreak 
    \unsect{5. Pixmap and cursor functions}
    \begin{itemize}
        \item Once you have connected to an X server, you can use the Xlib functions to:
            \begin{itemize}
                \item Create and free pixmaps
                \item Create, recolor, and free cursors
            \end{itemize}
        \item \textbf{Drawables}: In Xlib, a Drawable is a generic X resource that you can draw into or copy from.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The two main drawable resource types are
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            Window 
            Pixmap
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            In Xlib headers, they are all just X resource IDs:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                typedef XID Drawable;
                typedef XID Window;
                typedef XID Pixmap;
            \end{cppcode}
            Many drawing functions take a Drawable
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                XDrawLine(Display *display,
                    Drawable d,
                    GC gc,
                    int x1, int y1,
                    int x2, int y2
                );
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The \texttt{d} argument may be a window or a pixmap. A Window drawable represents an on-screen drawing destination. If you draw into a window, the result may appear on the display, subject to clipping, exposure, obscuring windows, child windows, and so on.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            A Pixmap drawable represents off-screen storage on the X server. You can draw into it, then later copy it into a window:
        \item \textbf{Creating and Freeing Pixmaps}: Pixmaps can only be used on the screen on which they were created. Pixmaps are off-screen resources that are used for various operations, such as defining cursors as tiling patterns or as the source for certain raster operations. Most graphics requests can operate either on a window or on a pixmap. A bitmap is a single bit-plane pixmap.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            To create a pixmap of a given size, use \textbf{XCreatePixmap.}
            \begin{cppcode}
                Pixmap XCreatePixmap(Display* display, Drawable d, unsigned int width, unsigned int height, unsigned int depth)
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item display Specifies the connection to the X server.
                \item d Specifies which screen the pixmap is created on.
                \item width, height Specify the width and height, which define the dimensions of the pixmap.
                \item depth Specifies the depth of the pixmap.
            \end{itemize}
            The \textbf{XCreatePixmap} function creates a pixmap of the width, height, and depth you specified and returns a pixmap ID that identifies it. It is valid to pass an \textbf{InputOnly} window to the drawable argument. The width and height arguments must be nonzero, or a \textbf{BadValue} error results. The depth argument must be one of the depths supported by the screen of the specified drawable, or a BadValue error results.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The server uses the specified drawable to determine on which screen to create the pixmap. The pixmap can be used only on this screen and only with other drawables of the same depth (see \textbf{XCopyPlane} for an exception to this rule). The initial contents of the pixmap are undefined. \textbf{XCreatePixmap} can generate BadAlloc, \textbf{BadDrawable,} and \textbf{BadValue} errors.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The \texttt{d} argument is important. You are not drawing into d; you are using it to tell Xlib/X server which screen the pixmap should be created on. Pixmaps can only be used on the screen where they were created.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            A Drawable specifies the screen because every drawable resource is already associated with a particular X screen inside the X server.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            To free all storage associated with a specified pixmap, use \textbf{XFreePixmap.}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                XFreePixmap (Display* display, Pixmap pixmap)
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item display Specifies the connection to the X server.
                \item pixmap Specifies the pixmap.
            \end{itemize}
            The \textbf{XFreePixmap} function first deletes the association between the pixmap ID and the pixmap. Then, the X server frees the pixmap storage when there are no references to it. The pixmap should never be referenced again.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \textbf{XFreePixmap} can generate a \textbf{BadPixmap} error.
        \item \textbf{Xlib cursors}: In Xlib, a cursor is not modeled as a modern full-color image. It is modeled as a small two-color bitmap cursor with optional transparency and a logical “active point.”
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            A cursor has four main parts:
            \begin{enumerate}
                \item source pixmap
                \item mask pixmap
                \item foreground/background colors
                \item hotspot
            \end{enumerate}
            The \textbf{source pixmap} is the actual 1-bit cursor image. Since it has depth 1, each pixel is either 0 or 1, not a full RGB color.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The \textbf{colors} tell X how to interpret those bits. Typically:
            \begin{itemize}
                \item source bit 1 -> foreground color
                \item source bit 0 -> background color
            \end{itemize}
            The \textbf{mask pixmap} controls which cursor pixels are visible. It must also have depth 1.
            \begin{itemize}
                \item mask bit 0 -> transparent / not part of cursor
                \item mask bit 1 -> visible cursor pixel
            \end{itemize}
            So the mask gives the cursor its outline or shape. Without a mask, the cursor would effectively be a full rectangle of source pixels. With a mask, only selected pixels are drawn as the cursor.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The \textbf{hotspot} is the coordinate inside the cursor image that counts as the actual pointer location.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            For example, with an arrow cursor, the visible image may be 16 by 16 pixels, but the actual click point is usually the arrow tip:
        \item \textbf{Fonts}: In old X11 terminology, a \textbf{font} is a server-side collection of \textbf{glyph bitmaps}. A glyph is just a small bitmap shape. Xlib can use those bitmap glyphs not only for drawing characters, but also for constructing cursors.
                \bigbreak \noindent 
                X provides a special built-in font named something like the cursor font. It contains predefined cursor glyphs: arrows, crosses, watches, hands, etc. The constants in:
                \bigbreak \noindent 
                \begin{cppcode}
                #include <X11/cursorfont.h>
                \end{cppcode}
                \bigbreak \noindent 
                Which contains
                \begin{itemize}
                    \item \textbf{XC\_X\_cursor}:	An “X” shaped cursor. Often used as a generic default or placeholder cursor.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_arrow}:	A generic arrow cursor.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_based\_arrow\_down}:	A downward-pointing arrow with a base/bar.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_based\_arrow\_up}:	An upward-pointing arrow with a base/bar.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_boat}:	A small boat-shaped cursor. Mostly decorative/legacy.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_bogosity}:	A strange “bad/invalid/weird” symbol. Mostly humorous/legacy.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_bottom\_left\_corner}:	Resize cursor for the bottom-left corner of a window.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_bottom\_right\_corner}:	Resize cursor for the bottom-right corner of a window.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_bottom\_side}:	Resize cursor for the bottom edge of a window.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_bottom\_tee}:	A T-shaped cursor associated with the bottom side.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_box\_spiral}:	A box/square spiral shape. Mostly decorative.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_center\_ptr}:	A pointer arrow whose hotspot is more centered than XC\_left\_ptr.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_circle}:	A circular cursor.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_clock}:	A clock-shaped cursor, usually indicating waiting/busy state.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_coffee\_mug}:	A coffee mug cursor. Mostly decorative/legacy.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_cross}:	A simple cross-shaped cursor.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_cross\_reverse}:	A reverse/inverted version of the cross cursor.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_crosshair}:	A precision crosshair cursor, often used for targeting or coordinate selection.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_diamond\_cross}:	A cross combined with a diamond-like shape.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_dot}:	A small dot cursor.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_dotbox}:	A dot inside a box. Useful for precise pointing.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_double\_arrow}:	A double-ended arrow, generally suggesting resizing or directional adjustment.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_draft\_large}:	A large drafting/drawing-style cursor.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_draft\_small}:	A smaller drafting/drawing-style cursor.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_draped\_box}:	A box-like cursor with a draped/covered appearance. Mostly decorative.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_exchange}:	An exchange/swap cursor, suggesting replacement or swapping.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_fleur}:	A four-direction move cursor, like arrows pointing up/down/left/right.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_gobbler}:	A “gobbler”/Pac-Man-like decorative cursor.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_gumby}:	A Gumby-like figure cursor. Decorative/legacy.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_hand1}:	A hand cursor, usually pointing or selecting.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_hand2}:	Another hand cursor variant. Commonly used for links or clickable things.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_heart}:	A heart-shaped cursor.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_icon}:	An icon-like cursor, historically associated with window/icon manipulation.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_iron\_cross}:	A heavy cross shape resembling an iron cross.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_left\_ptr}:	The usual left-leaning arrow pointer. This is the common default pointer.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_left\_side}:	Resize cursor for the left edge of a window.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_left\_tee}:	A T-shaped cursor associated with the left side.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_leftbutton}:	A mouse/button symbol emphasizing the left button.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_ll\_angle}:	Lower-left angle/corner shape.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_lr\_angle}:	Lower-right angle/corner shape.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_man}:	A small human figure cursor.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_middlebutton}:	A mouse/button symbol emphasizing the middle button.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_mouse}:	A mouse-shaped cursor.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_pencil}:	A pencil cursor, commonly used for drawing/editing.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_pirate}:	A pirate/skull-style cursor, often used for destructive or dangerous actions.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_plus}:	A plus-sign cursor. Often suggests adding, inserting, or selecting.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_question\_arrow}:	An arrow with a question mark, commonly used for help/context help.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_right\_ptr}:	A right-leaning or right-pointing pointer arrow.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_right\_side}:	Resize cursor for the right edge of a window.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_right\_tee}:	A T-shaped cursor associated with the right side.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_rightbutton}:	A mouse/button symbol emphasizing the right button.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_rtl\_logo}:	A stylized logo cursor. Rarely used in modern programs.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_sailboat}:	A sailboat-shaped cursor. Decorative/legacy.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_sb\_down\_arrow}:	Scrollbar-style down arrow.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_sb\_h\_double\_arrow}:	Horizontal double arrow, commonly suggesting horizontal resizing or scrolling.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_sb\_left\_arrow}:	Scrollbar-style left arrow.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_sb\_right\_arrow}:	Scrollbar-style right arrow.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_sb\_up\_arrow}:	Scrollbar-style up arrow.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_sb\_v\_double\_arrow}:	Vertical double arrow, commonly suggesting vertical resizing or scrolling.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_shuttle}:	A space-shuttle-shaped cursor. Decorative/legacy.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_sizing}:	Generic sizing/resizing cursor.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_spider}:	Spider-shaped cursor. Decorative/legacy.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_spraycan}:	Spray-can cursor, commonly associated with painting/drawing programs.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_star}:	Star-shaped cursor.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_target}:	Target/bullseye cursor.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_tcross}:	A T-like cross cursor.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_top\_left\_arrow}:	Arrow pointing toward the upper-left.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_top\_left\_corner}:	Resize cursor for the top-left corner of a window.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_top\_right\_corner}:	Resize cursor for the top-right corner of a window.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_top\_side}:	Resize cursor for the top edge of a window.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_top\_tee}:	A T-shaped cursor associated with the top side.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_trek}:	A Star-Trek-like insignia cursor. Decorative/legacy.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_ul\_angle}:	Upper-left angle/corner shape.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_umbrella}:	Umbrella-shaped cursor. Decorative/legacy.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_ur\_angle}:	Upper-right angle/corner shape.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_watch}:	Watch cursor, commonly used to indicate busy/waiting.
                    \item \textbf{XC\_xterm}:	Text insertion/I-beam cursor, commonly used over text areas.
                \end{itemize}
                The practical / common ones is the subset
                \begin{center}
                    \begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{@{}lXX@{}}
                        \toprule
                        \textbf{Cursor}&	\textbf{Description}&	\textbf{Common use} \\
                        \midrule
                        XC\_left\_ptr	&Standard arrow pointer, usually angled up-left.	&Default cursor for normal pointing and selection.\\[2ex]
                        XC\_xterm	&Text insertion cursor, usually an I-beam.	&Used over editable or selectable text regions.\\[2ex]
                        XC\_watch	&Watch/busy cursor.	&Indicates that the application is busy or waiting.\\[2ex]
                        XC\_hand1	&Hand-shaped cursor, first variant.	&Used for clickable or draggable objects.\\[2ex]
                        XC\_hand2	&Hand-shaped cursor, second variant.	&Commonly used for links or clickable UI elements.\\[2ex]
                        XC\_crosshair	&Crosshair cursor with horizontal and vertical lines.	&Precision pointing, drawing, targeting, coordinate selection.\\[2ex]
                        XC\_fleur	&Four-direction movement cursor.	&Moving windows, panels, objects, or selections.\\[2ex]
                        XC\_sb\_h\_double\_arrow	&Horizontal double-ended arrow.	&Horizontal resizing or horizontal scrollbar interaction.\\[2ex]
                        XC\_sb\_v\_double\_arrow	&Vertical double-ended arrow.	&Vertical resizing or vertical scrollbar interaction.\\[2ex]
                        XC\_top\_side	&Resize cursor for the top edge.	&Resizing a window or object from its top border.\\[2ex]
                        XC\_bottom\_side	&Resize cursor for the bottom edge.	&Resizing from the bottom border.\\[2ex]
                        XC\_left\_side	&Resize cursor for the left edge.	&Resizing from the left border.\\[2ex]
                        XC\_right\_side	&Resize cursor for the right edge.	&Resizing from the right border.\\[2ex]
                        XC\_top\_left\_corner&	Diagonal resize cursor for the top-left corner.	&Resizing from the upper-left corner.\\[2ex]
                        XC\_top\_right\_corner&	Diagonal resize cursor for the top-right corner.	&Resizing from the upper-right corner.\\[2ex]
                        XC\_bottom\_left\_corner&	Diagonal resize cursor for the bottom-left corner.	&Resizing from the lower-left corner.\\[2ex]
                        XC\_bottom\_right\_corner&	Diagonal resize cursor for the bottom-right corner.	&Resizing from the lower-right corner. \\
                        \bottomrule
                    \end{tabularx}
                \end{center}
        \item \textbf{Font}: In Xlib, Font is an X resource ID referring to a font loaded on the X server.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            It is usually defined conceptually like this:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            typedef XID font;
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            So Font is not a C struct containing glyph data directly. It is an integer-like handle that identifies a server-side font resource.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            For normal text drawing, a font contains glyphs for characters. For cursor creation, Xlib can also use those glyph bitmaps as cursor images.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            Font font = XLoadFont(display, "cursor");
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            This loads the standard X cursor font and returns a Font ID. Then XCreateGlyphCursor can use glyphs from that font as the cursor’s source and mask.
        \item \textbf{XColor}: XColor is a structure that represents a color in Xlib. It is commonly defined like this
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                typedef struct {
                    unsigned long pixel;
                    unsigned short red, green, blue;
                    char flags; 
                    char pad;
                } XColor;
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            These are 16-bit color components, not 8-bit components. So their values range from:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                0      // minimum intensity
                65535  // maximum intensity
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            pixel is the actual pixel value used by the X server for a color.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            It is not necessarily an RGB value in the ordinary sense. It is the value that can be placed into a drawable or into a GC foreground/background field.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The meaning of pixel depends on the visual and colormap.
        \item \textbf{Creating, Recoloring, and Freeing Cursors}:
            Each window can have a different cursor defined for it. Whenever the pointer is in a visible window, it is set to the cursor defined for that window. If no cursor was defined for that window, the cursor is the one defined for the parent window.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            From X's perspective, a cursor consists of a cursor source, mask, colors, and a hotspot. The mask pixmap determines the shape of the cursor and must be a depth of one. The source pixmap must have a depth of one, and the colors determine the colors of the source. The hotspot defines the point on the cursor that is reported when a pointer event occurs. There may be limitations imposed by the hardware on cursors as to size and whether a mask is implemented. \textbf{XQueryBestCursor} can be used to find out what sizes are possible. There is a standard font for creating cursors, but Xlib provides functions that you can use to create cursors from arbitrary fonts or from bitmaps.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            To create a cursor from the standard cursor font, use \textbf{XCreateFontCursor}.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            \begin{cppcode}
                #include <X11/cursorfont.h>

                Cursor XCreateFontCursor(Display *display, unsigned int shape);
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textit{display} specifies the connection to the X server.
                \item \textit{shape} specifies the shape of the cursor.
            \end{itemize}
            \bigbreak \noindent
            X provides a set of standard cursor shapes in a special font named \textit{cursor}. Applications are encouraged to use this interface for their cursors because they cannot customize for the individual display type. The \textit{shape} argument specifies which glyph of the standard fonts to use.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            The hotspot comes from the information stored in the cursor font. The initial colors of a cursor are a black foreground and a white background. See \textbf{XRecolorCursor}. For further information about cursor shapes, see appendix B.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            \textbf{XCreateFontCursor} can generate \textbf{BadAlloc} and \textbf{BadValue} errors.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            To create a cursor from font glyphs, use \textbf{XCreateGlyphCursor}.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            To create a cursor from font glyphs, use \textbf{XCreateGlyphCursor}.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            \begin{cppcode}
                Cursor XCreateGlyphCursor(
                    Display *display,
                    Font source_font,
                    Font mask_font,
                    unsigned int source_char,
                    unsigned int mask_char,
                    XColor *foreground_color,
                    XColor *background_color
                );
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textit{display} specifies the connection to the X server.
                \item \textit{source\_font} specifies the font for the source glyph.
                \item \textit{mask\_font} specifies the font for the mask glyph, or \textbf{None}.
                \item \textit{source\_char} specifies the character glyph for the source.
                \item \textit{mask\_char} specifies the glyph character for the mask.
                \item \textit{foreground\_color} specifies the RGB values for the foreground of the source.
                \item \textit{background\_color} specifies the RGB values for the background of the source.
            \end{itemize}
            \bigbreak \noindent
            The \textbf{XCreateGlyphCursor} function is similar to \textbf{XCreatePixmapCursor} except that the source and mask bitmaps are obtained from the specified font glyphs. The \textit{source\_char} must be a defined glyph in \textit{source\_font}, or a \textbf{BadValue} error results. If \textit{mask\_font} is given, \textit{mask\_char} must be a defined glyph in \textit{mask\_font}, or a \textbf{BadValue} error results. The \textit{mask\_font} and character are optional. The origins of the \textit{source\_char} and \textit{mask\_char}, if defined, glyphs are positioned coincidently and define the hotspot. The \textit{source\_char} and \textit{mask\_char} need not have the same bounding box metrics, and there is no restriction on the placement of the hotspot relative to the bounding boxes. If no \textit{mask\_char} is given, all pixels of the source are displayed. You can free the fonts immediately by calling \textbf{XFreeFont} if no further explicit references to them are to be made.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            For 2-byte matrix fonts, the 16-bit value should be formed with the \textit{byte1} member in the most significant byte and the \textit{byte2} member in the least significant byte.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            \textbf{XCreateGlyphCursor} can generate \textbf{BadAlloc}, \textbf{BadFont}, and \textbf{BadValue} errors.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \textbf{Note:} To understand, think of a font as a table of small bitmap drawings. Each drawing in the font is selected by a number. That number is what Xlib calls a “character,” even if it is not a normal letter.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            source_font + source_char
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            means: Use glyph number source\_char from source\_font as the cursor’s source image.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            mask_font + mask_char
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            means: Use glyph number mask\_char from mask\_font as the cursor’s transparency mask
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                source_font + source_char  -> choose the source glyph
                mask_font   + mask_char    -> choose the mask glyph
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The \textbf{source glyph} controls the cursor’s black/white pattern. Since it is a 1-bit glyph, each pixel is either 0 or 1
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                source bit 1 -> foreground color
                source bit 0 -> background color
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The mask glyph controls which pixels are visible at all.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                mask bit 1 -> show this cursor pixel
                mask bit 0 -> transparent pixel
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Essentially,
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                if mask bit is 0:
                    draw nothing

                if mask bit is 1:
                    if source bit is 1:
                        draw foreground color
                    else:
                        draw background color
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent
            To create a cursor from two bitmaps, use \textbf{XCreatePixmapCursor}.
            \begin{cppcode}
                Cursor XCreatePixmapCursor(
                    Display* display,
                    Pixmap source,
                    Pixmap mask,
                    XColor* foreground_color,
                    XColor* background_color,
                    unsigned int x, unsigned int y
                )
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{description}
                \item \textit{display} Specifies the connection to the X server.
                \item \textit{source} Specifies the shape of the source cursor.
                \item \textit{mask} Specifies the cursor source bits to be displayed or \textbf{None}.
                \item \textit{foreground\_color} Specifies the RGB values for the foreground of the source.
                \item \textit{background\_color} Specifies the RGB values for the background of the source.
                \item \textit{x}, \textit{y} Specify the $x$ and $y$ coordinates, which indicate the hotspot relative to the source's origin.
            \end{description}
            The \textbf{XCreatePixmapCursor} function creates a cursor and returns the cursor ID associated with it. The foreground and background RGB values must be specified using \textit{foreground\_color} and \textit{background\_color}, even if the X server only has \textbf{StaticGray} or \textbf{GrayScale} color. The foreground color is used for the pixels set to 1 in the source, and the background color is used for the pixels set to 0. Both source and mask, if specified, must have depth one, or a \textbf{BadMatch} error results, but can have any root. The mask argument defines the shape of the cursor. The pixels set to 1 in the mask define which source pixels are displayed, and the pixels set to 0 define which pixels are ignored. If no mask is given, all pixels of the source are displayed. The mask, if present, must be the same size as the pixmap defined by the source argument, or a \textbf{BadMatch} error results. The hotspot must be a point within the source, or a \textbf{BadMatch} error results.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The components of the cursor can be transformed arbitrarily to meet display limitations. The pixmaps can be freed immediately if no further explicit references to them are to be made. Subsequent drawing in the source or mask pixmap has an undefined effect on the cursor. The X server might or might not make a copy of the pixmap.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \textbf{XCreatePixmapCursor} can generate \textbf{BadAlloc} and \textbf{BadPixmap} errors.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            To determine useful cursor sizes, use \textbf{XQueryBestCursor}.
            \begin{cppcode}
                Status XQueryBestCursor(
                    Display* display,
                    Drawable d,
                    unsigned int width, height,
                    unsigned int* width_return, height_return
                )
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textit{display} Specifies the connection to the X server.
                \item \textit{d} Specifies the drawable, which indicates the screen.
                \item \textit{width}, \textit{height} Specify the width and height of the cursor that you want the size information for.
                \item \textit{width\_return}, \textit{height\_return} Return the best width and height that is closest to the specified width and height.
            \end{itemize}
            Some displays allow larger cursors than other displays. The \textbf{XQueryBestCursor} function provides a way to find out what size cursors are actually possible on the display. It returns the largest size that can be displayed. Applications should be prepared to use smaller cursors on displays that cannot support large ones.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \textbf{XQueryBestCursor} can generate a \textbf{BadDrawable} error.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            To change the color of a given cursor, use \textbf{XRecolorCursor.}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                XRecolorCursor (display, cursor, foreground_color, background_color)
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item display Specifies the connection to the X server.
                \item cursor Specifies the cursor.
                \item foreground\_color Specifies the RGB values for the foreground of the source.
                \item background\_color Specifies the RGB values for the background of the source.
            \end{itemize}
            The \textbf{XRecolorCursor} function changes the color of the specified cursor, and if the cursor is being displayed on a screen, the change is visible immediately. The pixel members of the \textbf{XColor} structures are ignored; only the RGB values are used.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \textbf{XRecolorCursor} can generate a BadCursor error.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            To free (destroy) a given cursor, use \textbf{XFreeCursor.}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                XFreeCursor (display, cursor)
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item display Specifies the connection to the X server.
                \item cursor Specifies the cursor.
            \end{itemize}
            The \textbf{XFreeCursor} function deletes the association between the cursor resource ID and the specified cursor. The cursor storage is freed when no other resource references it. The specified cursor ID should not be referred to again.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \textbf{XFreeCursor} can generate a BadCursor error.
    \end{itemize}

    \pagebreak 
    \unsect{6. Color management functions}
    \begin{itemize}
        \item \textbf{Intro}: Each X window always has an associated colormap that provides a level of indirection between pixel values and colors displayed on the screen. Xlib provides functions that you can use to manipulate a colormap. The X protocol defines colors using values in the RGB color space. The RGB color space is device dependent; rendering an RGB value on differing output devices typically results in different colors. Xlib also provides a means for clients to specify color using device-independent color spaces for consistent results across devices. Xlib supports device-independent color spaces derivable from the CIE XYZ color space. This includes the CIE XYZ, xyY, L*u*v*, and L*a*b* color spaces as well as the TekHVC color space. 
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            All functions, types, and symbols in this chapter with the prefix ‘‘Xcms’’ are defined in <X11/Xcms.h>. The remaining functions and types are defined in <X11/Xlib.h>.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Functions in this chapter manipulate the representation of color on the screen. For each possible value that a pixel can take in a window, there is a color cell in the colormap. For example, if a window is 4 bits deep, pixel values 0 through 15 are defined. A colormap is a collection of color cells. A color cell consists of a triple of red, green, and blue (RGB) values. The hardware imposes limits on the number of significant bits in these values. As each pixel is read out of display memory, the pixel is looked up in a colormap. The RGB value of the cell determines what color is displayed on the screen. On a grayscale display with a black-and-white monitor, the values are combined to determine the brightness on the screen.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Typically, an application allocates color cells or sets of color cells to obtain the desired colors. The client can allocate read-only cells. In which case, the pixel values for these colors can be shared among multiple applications, and the RGB value of the cell cannot be changed. If the client allocates read/write cells, they are exclusively owned by the client, and the color associated with the pixel value can be changed at will. Cells must be allocated (and, if read/write, initialized with an RGB value) by a client to obtain desired colors. The use of pixel value for an unallocated cell results in an undefined color.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Because colormaps are associated with windows, X supports displays with multiple colormaps and, indeed, different types of colormaps. If there are insufficient colormap resources in the display, some windows will display in their true colors, and others will display with incorrect colors. A window manager usually controls which windows are displayed in their true colors if more than one colormap is required for the color resources the applications are using. At any time, there is a set of installed colormaps for a screen. Windows using one of the installed colormaps display with true colors, and windows using other colormaps generally display with incorrect colors. You can control the set of installed colormaps by using \textbf{XInstallColormap} and \textbf{XUninstallColormap}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Colormaps are local to a particular screen. Screens always have a default colormap, and programs typically allocate cells out of this colormap. Generally, you should not write applications that monopolize color resources. Although some hardware supports multiple colormaps installed at one time, many of the hardware displays built today support only a single installed colormap, so the primitives are written to encourage sharing of colormap entries between applications.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The \textbf{DefaultColormap} macro returns the default colormap. The \textbf{DefaultVisual} macro returns the default visual type for the specified screen. Possible visual types are \textbf{StaticGray,} \textbf{GrayScale,} \textbf{StaticColor,} \textbf{PseudoColor,} \textbf{TrueColor,} or \textbf{DirectColor}
        \item \textbf{Color structures}: Functions that operate only on RGB color space values use an \textbf{XColor} structure, which contains:
            \begin{cppcode}
                typedef struct {
                    unsigned long pixel; /* pixel value */
                    unsigned short red, green, blue; /* rgb values */
                    char flags; /* DoRed, DoGreen, DoBlue */
                    char pad;
                } XColor;
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The red, green, and blue values are always in the range 0 to 65535 inclusive, independent of the number of bits actually used in the display hardware. The server scales these values down to the range used by the hardware. Black is represented by (0,0,0), and white is represented by (65535,65535,65535). In some functions, the flags member controls which of the red, green, and blue members is used and can be the inclusive OR of zero or more of \textbf{DoRed,} \textbf{DoGreen,} and \textbf{DoBlue}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Functions that operate on all color space values use an \textbf{XcmsColor} structure. This structure contains a union of substructures, each supporting color specification encoding for a particular color
            space. Like the \textbf{XColor} structure, the \textbf{XcmsColor} structure contains pixel and color specification information (the spec member in the XcmsColor structure).
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                typedef unsigned long XcmsColorFormat;/* Color Specification Format */
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                typedef struct {
                    union {
                        XcmsRGB RGB;
                        XcmsRGBi RGBi;
                        XcmsCIEXYZ CIEXYZ;
                        XcmsCIEuvY CIEuvY;
                        XcmsCIExyY CIExyY;
                        XcmsCIELab CIELab;
                        XcmsCIELuv CIELuv;
                        XcmsTekHVC TekHVC;
                        XcmsPad Pad;
                    } spec;
                    unsigned long pixel;
                    XcmsColorFormat format;
                } XcmsColor; /* Xcms Color Structure */
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Because the color specification can be encoded for the various color spaces, encoding for the spec member is identified by the format member, which is of type \textbf{XcmsColorFormat.} The following macros define standard formats.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                #define XcmsUndefinedFormat 0x00000000
                #define XcmsCIEXYZFormat 0x00000001 /* CIE XYZ */
                #define XcmsCIEuvYFormat 0x00000002 /* CIE u’v’Y */
                #define XcmsCIExyYFormat 0x00000003 /* CIE xyY */
                #define XcmsCIELabFormat 0x00000004 /* CIE L*a*b* */
                #define XcmsCIELuvFormat 0x00000005 /* CIE L*u*v* */
                #define XcmsTekHVCFormat 0x00000006 /* TekHVC */
                #define XcmsRGBFormat 0x80000000 /* RGB Device */
                #define XcmsRGBiFormat 0x80000001 /* RGB Intensity */
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Formats for device-independent color spaces are distinguishable from those for device-dependent spaces by the 32nd bit. If this bit is set, it indicates that the color specification is in a device-dependent form; otherwise, it is in a device-independent form. If the 31st bit is set, this indicates that the color space has been added to Xlib at run time (see section 6.12.4). The format value for a color space added at run time may be different each time the program is executed. If references to such a color space must be made outside the client (for example, storing a color specification in a file), then reference should be made by color space string prefix (see \textbf{XcmsFormatOfPrefix} and \textbf{XcmsPrefixOfFormat).}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Data types that describe the color specification encoding for the various color spaces are defined as follows:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                typedef double XcmsFloat;
                typedef struct {
                    unsigned short red; /* 0x0000 to 0xffff */
                    unsigned short green; /* 0x0000 to 0xffff */
                    unsigned short blue; /* 0x0000 to 0xffff */
                } XcmsRGB; /* RGB Device */

                typedef struct {
                    XcmsFloat red; /* 0.0 to 1.0 */
                    XcmsFloat green; /* 0.0 to 1.0 */
                    XcmsFloat blue; /* 0.0 to 1.0 */
                } XcmsRGBi; /* RGB Intensity */

                typedef struct {
                    XcmsFloat X;
                    XcmsFloat Y; /* 0.0 to 1.0 */
                    XcmsFloat Z;
                } XcmsCIEXYZ; /* CIE XYZ */

                typedef struct {
                    XcmsFloat u_prime; /* 0.0 to ˜0.6 */
                    XcmsFloat v_prime; /* 0.0 to ˜0.6 */
                    XcmsFloat Y; /* 0.0 to 1.0 */
                } XcmsCIEuvY; /* CIE u’v’Y */
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                typedef struct {
                    XcmsFloat x; /* 0.0 to ˜.75 */
                    XcmsFloat y; /* 0.0 to ˜.85 */
                    XcmsFloat Y; /* 0.0 to 1.0 */
                } XcmsCIExyY; /* CIE xyY */

                typedef struct {
                    XcmsFloat L_star; /* 0.0 to 100.0 */
                    XcmsFloat a_star;
                    XcmsFloat b_star;
                } XcmsCIELab; /* CIE L*a*b* */

                typedef struct {
                    XcmsFloat L_star; /* 0.0 to 100.0 */
                    XcmsFloat u_star;
                    XcmsFloat v_star;
                } XcmsCIELuv; /* CIE L*u*v* */

                typedef struct {
                    XcmsFloat H; /* 0.0 to 360.0 */
                    XcmsFloat V; /* 0.0 to 100.0 */
                    XcmsFloat C; /* 0.0 to 100.0 */
                } XcmsTekHVC; /* TekHVC */

                typedef struct {
                    XcmsFloat pad0;
                    XcmsFloat pad1;
                    XcmsFloat pad2;
                    XcmsFloat pad3;
                } XcmsPad; /* four doubles */
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The device-dependent formats provided allow color specification in:
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textbf{RGB Intensity (XcmsRGBi)}: Red, green, and blue linear intensity values, floating-point values from 0.0 to 1.0, where 1.0 indicates full intensity, 0.5 half intensity, and so on.
                \item \textbf{RGB Device (XcmsRGB)}: Red, green, and blue values appropriate for the specified output device. \textbf{XcmsRGB} values are of type unsigned short, scaled from 0 to 65535 inclusive, and are interchangeable with the red, green, and blue values in an \textbf{XColor} structure.
            \end{itemize}
            It is important to note that RGB Intensity values are not gamma corrected values. In contrast, RGB Device values generated as a result of converting color specifications are always gamma corrected, and RGB Device values acquired as a result of querying a colormap or passed in by the client are assumed by Xlib to be gamma corrected. The term RGB value in this manual always refers to an RGB Device value.
        \item \textbf{Display color gamut}: Specific range of colors a screen can reproduce.
        \item \textbf{Gamma correction}:  Gamma correction is the process of adjusting color or brightness values to account for the fact that displays do not produce light linearly.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Ideally, you might think that if you send a display a value of 50\%, it produces 50\% brightness. In reality, many displays have a nonlinear response. A value halfway between black and white does not necessarily produce half the physical light output.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            So gamma correction compensates for this nonlinear relationship.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Mathematically, it is often modeled like this:
            \begin{align*}
                \text{output brightness } = \text{ input value}^{\gamma}
            ,\end{align*}
            where $\gamma$ is the \textbf{gamma value}. A common display gamma is around 
            \begin{align*}
                \gamma \approx 2.2
            .\end{align*}
            That means displays often make raw input values appear darker than a linear brightness model would suggest.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            For example, suppose a pixel channel is normalized between 0 and 1:
            \begin{align*}
                0.5^{2.2} \approx 0.218
            .\end{align*}
            So a numeric value of 0.5 may produce only about 21.8\% of maximum physical light output, not 50\%.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Gamma correction adjusts the stored or transmitted values so the displayed result looks perceptually correct.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            In color systems, this matters because RGB values are usually not pure physical light intensities. They are often encoded values meant to look correct on a display. For example, an RGB value like:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            R = 128, G = 128, B = 128
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            is not necessarily “half the light” of white. It is a gamma-encoded middle gray intended to look visually halfway between black and white.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Gamma correcting an RGB value means changing the numeric channel values so that the final displayed brightness matches the intended perceived or physical brightness.
        \item \textbf{Color strings}: Xlib provides a mechanism for using string names for colors. A color string may either contain an abstract color name or a numerical color specification. Color strings are case-insensitive.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Color strings are used in the following functions:
            \begin{itemize}
                \item XAllocNamedColor
                \item XcmsAllocNamedColor
                \item XLookupColor
                \item XcmsLookupColor
                \item XParseColor
                \item XStoreNamedColor
            \end{itemize}
            Xlib supports the use of abstract color names, for example, red or blue. A value for this abstract name is obtained by searching one or more color name databases. Xlib first searches zero or more client-side databases; the number, location, and content of these databases is implementation-dependent and might depend on the current locale. If the name is not found, Xlib then looks for the color in the X server’s database. If the color name is not in the Host Portable Character Encoding, the result is implementation-dependent.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            A numerical color specification consists of a color space name and a set of values in the following syntax:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            <color_space_name>:<value>/.../<value>
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The following are examples of valid color strings.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{itemize}
                \item "CIEXYZ:0.3227/0.28133/0.2493"
                \item "RGBi:1.0/0.0/0.0"
                \item "rgb:00/ff/00"
                \item "CIELuv:50.0/0.0/0.0"
            \end{itemize}
            The syntax and semantics of numerical specifications are given for each standard color space in the following sections.
        \item \textbf{RGB Device String Specification}: An RGB Device specification is identified by the prefix ‘‘rgb:’’ and conforms to the following syntax:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            rgb:<red>/<green>/<blue>

            <red>, <green>, <blue> := h | hh | hhh | hhhh
            h := single hexadecimal digits (case insignificant)
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Note that $h$ indicates the value scaled in 4 bits, $hh$ the value scaled in 8 bits, $hhh$ the value scaled in 12 bits, and hhhh the value scaled in 16 bits, respectively.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            For backward compatibility, an older syntax for RGB Device is supported, but its continued use is not encouraged. The syntax is an initial sharp sign character followed by a numeric specification, in one of the following formats:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                #RGB (4 bits each)
                #RRGGBB (8 bits each)
                #RRRGGGBBB (12 bits each)
                #RRRRGGGGBBBB (16 bits each)
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The R, G, and B represent single hexadecimal digits. When fewer than 16 bits each are specified, they represent the most significant bits of the value (unlike the ‘‘rgb:’’ syntax, in which values are scaled). For example, the string ‘‘\#3a7’’ is the same as ‘‘\#3000a0007000’’.
        \item \textbf{RGB Intensity String Specification}: An RGB intensity specification is identified by the prefix ‘‘rgbi:’’ and conforms to the following syntax:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            rgbi:<red>/<green>/<blue>
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Note that red, green, and blue are floating-point values between 0.0 and 1.0, inclusive. The input format for these values is an optional sign, a string of numbers possibly containing a decimal point, and an optional exponent field containing an E or e followed by a possibly signed integer string.
        \item \textbf{Device-Independent String Specifications}: The standard device-independent string speciﬁcations have the following syntax:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
                CIEXYZ:<X>/<Y>/<Z>
                CIEuvY:<u>/<v>/<Y>
                CIExyY:<x>/<y>/<Y>
                CIELab:<L>/<a>/<b>
                CIELuv:<L>/<u>/<v>
                TekHVC:<H>/<V>/<C>
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            All of the values (C, H, V, X, Y, Z, a, b, u, v, y, x) are floating-point values. The syntax for these values is an optional plus or minus sign, a string of digits possibly containing a decimal point, and an optional exponent field consisting of an ‘‘E’’ or ‘‘e’’ followed by an optional plus or minus followed by a string of digits.
        \item \textbf{Color Conversion Contexts and Gamut Mapping}: When Xlib converts device-independent color specifications into device-dependent specifications and vice versa, it uses knowledge about the color limitations of the screen hardware. This information, typically called the device profile, is available in a Color Conversion Context (CCC).
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Because a specified color may be outside the color gamut of the target screen and the white point associated with the color specification may differ from the white point inherent to the screen, Xlib applies gamut mapping when it encounters certain conditions:
            \begin{itemize}
                \item Gamut compression occurs when conversion of device-independent color specifications to device-dependent color specifications results in a color out of the target screen’s gamut.
                \item White adjustment occurs when the inherent white point of the screen differs from the white point assumed by the client.
            \end{itemize}
            Gamut handling methods are stored as callbacks in the CCC, which in turn are used by the color space conversion routines. Client data is also stored in the CCC for each callback. The CCC also contains the white point the client assumes to be associated with color specifications (that is, the Client White Point). The client can specify the gamut handling callbacks and client data as well as the Client White Point. Xlib does not preclude the X client from performing other forms of gamut handling (for example, gamut expansion); however, Xlib does not provide direct support for gamut handling other than white adjustment and gamut compression.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Associated with each colormap is an initial CCC transparently generated by Xlib. Therefore, when you specify a colormap as an argument to an Xlib function, you are indirectly specifying a CCC. There is a default CCC associated with each screen. Newly created CCCs inherit attributes from the default CCC, so the default CCC attributes can be modified to affect new CCCs.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            Xcms functions in which gamut mapping can occur return \textbf{Status} and have specific status values defined for them, as follows:
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textbf{XcmsFailure} indicates that the function failed.
                \item \textbf{XcmsSuccess} indicates that the function succeeded. In addition, if the function performed any color conversion, the colors did not need to be compressed.
                \item \textbf{XcmsSuccessWithCompression} indicates the function performed color conversion and at least one of the colors needed to be compressed. The gamut compression method is determined by the gamut compression procedure in the CCC that is specified directly as a function argument or in the CCC indirectly specified by means of the colormap argument.
            \end{itemize}
        \item \textbf{Creating, Copying, and Destroying Colormaps}: 
            \bigbreak \noindent
            To create a colormap for a screen, use \textbf{XCreateColormap}.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            \begin{cppcode}
                Colormap XCreateColormap(
                    Display *display,
                    Window w,
                    Visual *visual,
                    int alloc
                )
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textbf{display}: Specifies the connection to the X server.
                \item \textbf{w}: Specifies the window on whose screen you want to create a colormap.
                \item \textbf{visual}: Specifies a visual type supported on the screen. If the visual type is not one supported by the screen, a \textbf{BadMatch} error results.
                \item \textbf{alloc}: Specifies the colormap entries to be allocated. You can pass \textbf{AllocNone} or \textbf{AllocAll}.
            \end{itemize}
            \bigbreak \noindent
            The \textbf{XCreateColormap} function creates a colormap of the specified visual type for the screen on which the specified window resides and returns the colormap ID associated with it. Note that the specified window is only used to determine the screen.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            The initial values of the colormap entries are undefined for the visual classes \textbf{GrayScale}, \textbf{PseudoColor}, and \textbf{DirectColor}. For \textbf{StaticGray}, \textbf{StaticColor}, and \textbf{TrueColor}, the entries have defined values, but those values are specific to the visual and are not defined by X. For \textbf{StaticGray}, \textbf{StaticColor}, and \textbf{TrueColor}, \textbf{alloc} must be \textbf{AllocNone}, or a \textbf{BadMatch} error results. For the other visual classes, if \textbf{alloc} is \textbf{AllocNone}, the colormap initially has no allocated entries, and clients can allocate them. 
            \bigbreak \noindent
            If \textbf{alloc} is \textbf{AllocAll}, the entire colormap is allocated writable. The initial values of all allocated entries are undefined. For \textbf{GrayScale} and \textbf{PseudoColor}, the effect is as if an \textbf{XAllocColorCells} call returned all pixel values from zero to $N - 1$, where $N$ is the colormap entries value in the specified visual. For \textbf{DirectColor}, the effect is as if an \textbf{XAllocColorPlanes} call returned a pixel value of zero and \texttt{red\_mask}, \texttt{green\_mask}, and \texttt{blue\_mask} containing the same bits as the corresponding masks in the specified visual. However, in all cases, none of these entries can be freed by using \textbf{XFreeColors}.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            \textbf{XCreateColormap} can generate \textbf{BadAlloc}, \textbf{BadMatch}, \textbf{BadValue}, and \textbf{BadWindow} errors.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            To create a new colormap when the allocation out of a previously shared colormap has failed because of resource exhaustion, use \textbf{XCopyColormapAndFree}.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            \begin{cppcode}
                Colormap XCopyColormapAndFree(
                    Display* display,
                    Colormap colormap
                )
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textbf{display}: Specifies the connection to the X server.
                \item \textbf{colormap}: Specifies the colormap.
            \end{itemize}
            \bigbreak \noindent
            The \textbf{XCopyColormapAndFree} function creates a colormap of the same visual type and for the same screen as the specified colormap and returns the new colormap ID. It also moves all of the client's existing allocation from the specified colormap to the new colormap with their color values intact and their read-only or writable characteristics intact and frees those entries in the specified colormap. Color values in other entries in the new colormap are undefined.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            If the specified colormap was created by the client with \textbf{alloc} not set to \textbf{AllocAll}, the new colormap is also created with \textbf{AllocAll}, all color values for all entries are copied from the specified colormap, and then all entries in the specified colormap are freed.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            If the specified colormap was not created by the client with \textbf{AllocAll}, the allocations to be moved are all those pixels and planes that have been allocated by the client using \textbf{XAllocColor}, \textbf{XAllocNamedColor}, \textbf{XAllocColorCells}, or \textbf{XAllocColorPlanes} and that have not been freed since they were allocated.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            \textbf{XCopyColormapAndFree} can generate \textbf{BadAlloc} and \textbf{BadColor} errors.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \textbf{Note:} A colormap can be “exhausted” because it has a finite number of color cells.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            If the visual has 8 colormap bits, there may be only 256 entries. Multiple clients may be sharing the same default colormap. If enough clients allocate entries, there may be no suitable entries left.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The “AndFree” part means it frees your color allocations in the old colormap. It does not necessarily destroy the old colormap object itself. To destroy a colormap resource, you use:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            If the old colormap was created by your client with:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \begin{cppcode}
            AllocAll
            \end{cppcode}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            then the client owns the entire colormap. In that case, the new colormap is also treated like an \textbf{AllocAll} colormap. All entries are copied, and the old entries are freed.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            If the old colormap was not created with \textbf{AllocAll,} then only the colors/cells that your client explicitly allocated are moved. That includes allocations made by functions such as:
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \textbf{XCopyColormapAndFree} creates a new colormap with the same screen, same visual, and same number of entries as the old one. It does not increase the size of the colormap.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            For example, if the old colormap had 256 entries, the new colormap also has 256 entries.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The benefit is that the new colormap does not carry over everyone else’s allocations. It mainly preserves your client’s allocated colors and leaves the rest available/undefined in the new colormap.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            To destroy a colormap, use \textbf{XFreeColormap}.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            \begin{cppcode}
                XFreeColormap(Display *display, Colormap colormap)
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textbf{display}: Specifies the connection to the X server.
                \item \textbf{colormap}: Specifies the colormap that you want to destroy.
            \end{itemize}
            \bigbreak \noindent
            The \textbf{XFreeColormap} function deletes the association between the colormap resource ID and the colormap and frees the colormap storage. However, this function has no effect on the default colormap for a screen. If the specified colormap is an installed map for a screen, it is uninstalled. See \textbf{XUninstallColormap}.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            If the specified colormap is defined as the colormap for a window by \textbf{XCreateWindow}, \textbf{XSetWindowColormap}, or \textbf{XChangeWindowAttributes}, \textbf{XFreeColormap} changes the colormap associated with the window to \textbf{None} and generates a \textbf{ColormapNotify} event. X does not define the colors displayed for a window with a colormap of \textbf{None}.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            \textbf{XFreeColormap} can generate a \textbf{BadColor} error.
        \item \textbf{Mapping Color Names to Values}: To map a color name to an RGB value, use \textbf{XLookupColor}
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            \bigbreak \noindent
            \begin{cppcode}
                Status XLookupColor(
                    Display *display,
                    Colormap colormap,
                    char *color_name,
                    XColor *exact_def_return,
                    XColor *screen_def_return
                )
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textbf{display}: Specifies the connection to the X server.
                \item \textbf{colormap}: Specifies the colormap.
                \item \textbf{color\_name}: Specifies the color name string, for example, \texttt{red}, whose color definition structure you want returned.
                \item \textbf{exact\_def\_return}: Returns the exact RGB values.
                \item \textbf{screen\_def\_return}: Returns the closest RGB values provided by the hardware.
            \end{itemize}
            \bigbreak \noindent
            The \textbf{XLookupColor} function looks up the string name of a color with respect to the screen associated with the specified colormap. It returns both the exact color values and the closest values provided by the screen with respect to the visual type of the specified colormap. If the color name is not in the Host Portable Character Encoding, the result is implementation-dependent. Use of uppercase or lowercase does not matter. \textbf{XLookupColor} returns nonzero if the name is resolved; otherwise, it returns zero.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            \textbf{XLookupColor} can generate a \textbf{BadColor} error.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            To map a color name to the exact RGB value, use \textbf{XParseColor}.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            \begin{cppcode}
                Status XParseColor(Display *display, Colormap colormap, char *spec,
                XColor *exact_def_return)
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textbf{display}: Specifies the connection to the X server.
                \item \textbf{colormap}: Specifies the colormap.
                \item \textbf{spec}: Specifies the color name string; case is ignored.
                \item \textbf{exact\_def\_return}: Returns the exact color value for later use and sets the \textbf{DoRed}, \textbf{DoGreen}, and \textbf{DoBlue} flags.
            \end{itemize}
            \bigbreak \noindent
            The \textbf{XParseColor} function looks up the string name of a color with respect to the screen associated with the specified colormap. It returns the exact color value. If the color name is not in the Host Portable Character Encoding, the result is implementation-dependent. Use of uppercase or lowercase does not matter. \textbf{XParseColor} returns nonzero if the name is resolved; otherwise, it returns zero.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            \textbf{XParseColor} can generate a \textbf{BadColor} error.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            To map a color name to a value in an arbitrary color space, use \textbf{XcmsLookupColor}.
            \bigbreak \noindent
            \begin{cppcode}
                Status XcmsLookupColor(
                    Display *display,
                    Colormap colormap,
                    char *color_string,
                    XcmsColor *color_exact_return,
                    XcmsColor *color_screen_return,
                    XcmsColorFormat result_format
                )
            \end{cppcode}
            \begin{itemize}
                \item \textbf{display}: Specifies the connection to the X server.
                \item \textbf{colormap}: Specifies the colormap.
                \item \textbf{color\_string}: Specifies the color string.
                \item \textbf{color\_exact\_return}: Returns the color specification parsed from the color string or parsed from the corresponding string found in a color-name database.
                \item \textbf{color\_screen\_return}: Returns the color that can be reproduced on the screen.
                \item \textbf{result\_format}: Specifies the color format for the returned color specifications, that is, the \texttt{color\_screen\_return} and \texttt{color\_exact\_return} arguments. If the format is \textbf{XcmsUndefinedFormat} and the color string contains a numerical color specification, the specification is returned in the format used in that numerical color specification. If the format is \textbf{XcmsUndefinedFormat} and the color string contains a color name, the specification is returned in the format used to store the color in the database.
            \end{itemize}
            \bigbreak \noindent
            The \textbf{XcmsLookupColor} function looks up the string name of a color with respect to the screen associated with the specified colormap. It returns both the exact color values and the closest values provided by the screen with respect to the visual type of the specified colormap. The values are returned in the format specified by \texttt{result\_format}. If the color name is not in the Host Portable Character Encoding, the result is implementation-dependent. Use of uppercase or lowercase does not matter. \textbf{XcmsLookupColor} returns \textbf{XcmsSuccess} or \textbf{XcmsSuccessWithCompression} if the name is resolved; otherwise, it returns \textbf{XcmsFailure}. If \textbf{XcmsSuccessWithCompression} is returned, the color specification returned in \texttt{color\_screen\_return} is the result of gamut compression.
        \item \textbf{Clearing up confusion}: The colormap is stored and managed by the X server, not by the client.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The client only holds an XID, a numeric handle, that refers to the server-side colormap object.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            This is why multiple clients can share the same colormap. They are all referring to the same server-side resource by XID.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            For a client to allocate a read-only color cell means: The client asks the X server for a colormap entry representing some RGB color, and the server gives the client a pixel value it may use, but not modify.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The important part is that the client has allocated permission to use that pixel value, not permission to change the RGB contents of the colormap cell.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            The word allocate here means the server records that this client is using that shared color cell. It increments an internal count so the entry is not freed while clients are still using it.
            \bigbreak \noindent 
            When clients call \textbf{XFreeColors,} the server decrements that count. The cell is actually available for reuse only after all allocations have been freed.
        \item \textbf{Allocating and Freeing Color Cells}: There are two ways of allocating color cells: explicitly as read-only entries, one pixel value at a time, or read/write, where you can allocate a number of color cells and planes simultaneously. A read-only cell has its RGB value set by the server. Read/write cells do not have defined colors initially; functions described in the next section must be used to store values into them. Although it is possible for any client to store values into a read/write cell allocated by another client, read/write cells normally should be considered private to the client that allocated them.
            \bigbreak \noindent
